From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia

Rapidly rotating storm system

Several terms of bands and albums redirect here. For other uses, see [Tropical Depression (band)](/wiki/Tropical_Depression_(band) "Tropical Depression (band)"), [The Mountain Goats discography § Split EPs](/wiki/The_Mountain_Goats_discography#Split_EPs "The Mountain Goats discography"), and [Tropical Storm (album)](/wiki/Tropical_Storm_(album) "Tropical Storm (album)").

For [technical reasons](/wiki/Wikipedia:Naming_conventions_(technical_restrictions) "Wikipedia:Naming conventions (technical restrictions)"), "Hurricane #1" redirects here. For the band, see [Hurricane No. 1](/wiki/Hurricane_No._1 "Hurricane No. 1").

"Hurricane" redirects here. For other uses, see [Hurricane (disambiguation)](/wiki/Hurricane_(disambiguation) "Hurricane (disambiguation)").

[![Space view of a tropical cyclone with a well-defined eye](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Dramatic_Views_of_Hurricane_Florence_from_the_International_Space_Station_From_9_12_%2842828603210%29_%28cropped%29.jpg/500px-Dramatic_Views_of_Hurricane_Florence_from_the_International_Space_Station_From_9_12_%2842828603210%29_%28cropped%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Dramatic_Views_of_Hurricane_Florence_from_the_International_Space_Station_From_9_12_(42828603210)_(cropped).jpg)

[Hurricane Florence](/wiki/Hurricane_Florence "Hurricane Florence") viewed from the [International Space Station](/wiki/International_Space_Station "International Space Station") in 2018. The [eye](/wiki/Eye_(cyclone) "Eye (cyclone)"), eyewall, and surrounding [rainbands](/wiki/Rainband "Rainband") are characteristics of tropical cyclones.

A **tropical cyclone** is a rapidly rotating [storm system](/wiki/Storm_system "Storm system") with a [low-pressure area](/wiki/Low-pressure_area "Low-pressure area"), a closed low-level [atmospheric circulation](/wiki/Atmospheric_circulation "Atmospheric circulation"), strong winds, and a spiral arrangement of [thunderstorms](/wiki/Thunderstorm "Thunderstorm") that produce heavy rain and [squalls](/wiki/Squall "Squall"). Depending on its location and strength, a tropical cyclone is called a **hurricane** (), **typhoon** (), **tropical storm**, **cyclonic storm**, **tropical depression**, or simply **cyclone**. A hurricane is a strong tropical cyclone that occurs in the [Atlantic Ocean](/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean "Atlantic Ocean") or northeastern [Pacific Ocean](/wiki/Pacific_Ocean "Pacific Ocean"). A [typhoon](/wiki/Typhoon "Typhoon") is the same thing which occurs in the northwestern Pacific Ocean. In the [Indian Ocean](/wiki/Indian_Ocean "Indian Ocean") and South Pacific, comparable storms are referred to as "tropical cyclones". In modern times, on average around 80 to 90 named tropical cyclones form each year around the world, over half of which develop [hurricane-force winds](/wiki/Hurricane-force_winds "Hurricane-force winds") of 65 [kn](/wiki/Knot_(unit) "Knot (unit)") (120 km/h; 75 mph) or more.[[1]](#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-1)

Tropical cyclones [typically form](/wiki/Tropical_cyclogenesis "Tropical cyclogenesis") over large bodies of relatively warm water. They derive their energy through the evaporation of [water](/wiki/Water "Water") from the [ocean](/wiki/Ocean "Ocean") surface, which ultimately [condenses](/wiki/Condenses "Condenses") into [clouds](/wiki/Cloud "Cloud") and rain when moist air rises and cools to [saturation](/wiki/Saturated_fluid "Saturated fluid"). This [energy source](/wiki/Energy_source "Energy source") differs from that of [mid-latitude cyclonic storms](/wiki/Extratropical_cyclone "Extratropical cyclone"), such as [nor'easters](/wiki/Nor%27easter "Nor'easter") and [European windstorms](/wiki/European_windstorm "European windstorm"), which are powered primarily by [horizontal temperature contrasts](/wiki/Baroclinic_instability "Baroclinic instability"). Tropical cyclones are typically between 100 and 2,000 km (62 and 1,243 mi) in diameter. The strong rotating winds of a tropical cyclone are a result of the [conservation of angular momentum](/wiki/Conservation_of_angular_momentum "Conservation of angular momentum") imparted by the [Earth's rotation](/wiki/Earth%27s_rotation "Earth's rotation") as air flows inwards toward the axis of rotation. As a result, cyclones rarely form within 5° of the [equator](/wiki/Equator "Equator"). [South Atlantic tropical cyclones](/wiki/South_Atlantic_tropical_cyclone "South Atlantic tropical cyclone") are very rare due to consistently strong [wind shear](/wiki/Wind_shear "Wind shear") and a weak [Intertropical Convergence Zone](/wiki/Intertropical_Convergence_Zone "Intertropical Convergence Zone"). In contrast, the [African easterly jet](/wiki/African_easterly_jet "African easterly jet") and areas of [atmospheric instability](/wiki/Atmospheric_instability "Atmospheric instability") give rise to cyclones in the Atlantic Ocean and [Caribbean Sea](/wiki/Caribbean_Sea "Caribbean Sea").

Heat energy from the ocean acts as the accelerator for tropical cyclones. This causes inland regions to suffer far less damage from cyclones than coastal regions, although the impacts of flooding are felt across the board. Coastal damage may be caused by strong winds and rain, high waves, [storm surges](/wiki/Storm_surge "Storm surge"), and [tornadoes](/wiki/Tornado "Tornado"). [Climate change affects tropical cyclones](/wiki/Tropical_cyclones_and_climate_change "Tropical cyclones and climate change") in several ways. Scientists have found that climate change can exacerbate the impact of tropical cyclones by increasing their duration, occurrence, and intensity due to the [warming of ocean waters](/wiki/Ocean_temperature "Ocean temperature") and [intensification of the water cycle](/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_the_water_cycle "Effects of climate change on the water cycle").[[2]](#cite_note-:5-2)[[3]](#cite_note-auto-3) Tropical cyclones draw in air from a large area and concentrate the water content of that air into [precipitation](/wiki/Precipitation "Precipitation") over a much smaller area. This replenishing of moisture-bearing air after rain may cause multi-hour or multi-day extremely heavy rain up to 40 km (25 mi) from the coastline, far beyond the amount of water that the local atmosphere holds at any one time. This in turn can lead to river [flooding](/wiki/Flooding "Flooding"), overland flooding, and a general overwhelming of local water control structures across a large area.

## Definition and terminology

A tropical cyclone is the generic term for a warm-cored, non-frontal [synoptic-scale](/wiki/Synoptic_scale_meteorology "Synoptic scale meteorology") [low-pressure system](/wiki/Low-pressure_system "Low-pressure system") over [tropical](/wiki/Tropical "Tropical") or [subtropical](/wiki/Subtropical "Subtropical") waters around the world.[[4]](#cite_note-Gloss-4)[[5]](#cite_note-What-5) The systems generally have a well-defined center which is surrounded by deep [atmospheric convection](/wiki/Atmospheric_convection "Atmospheric convection") and a closed wind circulation at the surface.[[4]](#cite_note-Gloss-4) A tropical cyclone is generally deemed to have formed once mean surface winds in excess of 35 kn (65 km/h; 40 mph) are observed.[[1]](#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-1) It is assumed at this stage that a tropical cyclone has become self-sustaining and can continue to intensify without any help from its environment.[[1]](#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-1)

Depending on its location and strength, a tropical cyclone is referred to by [different names](#Classification_and_naming), including *hurricane*, *typhoon*, *tropical storm*, *cyclonic storm*, *tropical depression*, or simply *cyclone*. A [hurricane](/wiki/Atlantic_hurricane "Atlantic hurricane") is a strong tropical cyclone that occurs in the [Atlantic Ocean](/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean "Atlantic Ocean") or northeastern [Pacific Ocean](/wiki/Pacific_Ocean "Pacific Ocean"), and a [typhoon](/wiki/Typhoon "Typhoon") occurs in the northwestern Pacific Ocean. In the [Indian Ocean](/wiki/Indian_Ocean "Indian Ocean") and South Pacific, comparable storms are referred to as "tropical cyclones", and such storms in the Indian Ocean can also be called "severe cyclonic storms".

*Tropical* refers to the geographical origin of these systems, which form almost exclusively over [tropical](/wiki/Tropical "Tropical") seas. *[Cyclone](/wiki/Cyclone "Cyclone")* refers to their winds moving in a circle, whirling round their central clear [eye](/wiki/Eye_(cyclone) "Eye (cyclone)"), with their surface winds blowing [counterclockwise](/wiki/Counterclockwise "Counterclockwise") in the [Northern Hemisphere](/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere "Northern Hemisphere") and [clockwise](/wiki/Clockwise "Clockwise") in the [Southern Hemisphere](/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere "Southern Hemisphere"). The opposite direction of circulation is due to the [Coriolis effect](/wiki/Coriolis_effect "Coriolis effect").

## Formation

Main article: [Tropical cyclogenesis](/wiki/Tropical_cyclogenesis "Tropical cyclogenesis")

[![A schematic diagram of a tropical cyclone](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Hurricane-en.svg/330px-Hurricane-en.svg.png)](/wiki/File:Hurricane-en.svg)

A diagram of a tropical cyclone in the [Northern Hemisphere](/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere "Northern Hemisphere")

Tropical cyclones tend to develop during the summer, but have been noted in nearly every month in most [tropical cyclone basins](/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_basins "Tropical cyclone basins"). Tropical cyclones on either side of the Equator generally have their origins in the [Intertropical Convergence Zone](/wiki/Intertropical_Convergence_Zone "Intertropical Convergence Zone") (ITCZ), where winds blow from either the northeast or southeast.[[6]](#cite_note-Form-6) Within this broad area of low-pressure, air is heated over the warm tropical ocean and rises in discrete parcels, which causes towering thunderstorms to form.[[6]](#cite_note-Form-6) These showers dissipate quite quickly; however, they can group together into large clusters of thunderstorms.[[6]](#cite_note-Form-6) This creates a flow of warm, moist, rapidly rising air, which starts to [rotate cyclonically](/wiki/Cyclonic_rotation "Cyclonic rotation") as it interacts with the rotation of the earth.[[6]](#cite_note-Form-6)

Several factors are required for these thunderstorms to develop further, including [sea surface temperatures](/wiki/Sea_surface_temperatures "Sea surface temperatures") of around 27 °C (81 °F) and low vertical [wind shear](/wiki/Wind_shear "Wind shear") surrounding the system,[[6]](#cite_note-Form-6)[[7]](#cite_note-A15-7) atmospheric instability, high [humidity](/wiki/Humidity "Humidity") in the lower to middle levels of the [troposphere](/wiki/Troposphere "Troposphere"), enough [Coriolis force](/wiki/Coriolis_force "Coriolis force") to develop a [low-pressure center](/wiki/Low-pressure_area "Low-pressure area"), and a pre-existing low-level focus or disturbance.[[7]](#cite_note-A15-7)
There is a limit on tropical cyclone intensity which is strongly related to the water temperatures along its path.[[8]](#cite_note-RSMAS-8) and upper-level divergence.[[9]](#cite_note-9)
An average of 86 tropical cyclones of tropical storm intensity form annually worldwide. Of those cyclones, 47 reach strengths higher than 119 km/h (74 mph), and 20 become intense tropical cyclones, of at least Category 3 intensity on the [Saffir–Simpson scale](/wiki/Saffir%E2%80%93Simpson_scale "Saffir–Simpson scale").[[10]](#cite_note-10)

Climate oscillations such as [El Niño–Southern Oscillation](/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o%E2%80%93Southern_Oscillation "El Niño–Southern Oscillation") (ENSO) and the [Madden–Julian oscillation](/wiki/Madden%E2%80%93Julian_oscillation "Madden–Julian oscillation") modulate the timing and frequency of tropical cyclone development.[[11]](#cite_note-AOML_climt_var-11)[[12]](#cite_note-12)[[13]](#cite_note-13)[[14]](#cite_note-14) [Rossby waves](/wiki/Rossby_wave "Rossby wave") can aid in the formation of a new tropical cyclone by disseminating the energy of an existing, mature storm.[[15]](#cite_note-15)[[16]](#cite_note-16) [Kelvin waves](/wiki/Kelvin_wave "Kelvin wave") can contribute to tropical cyclone formation by regulating the development of the [westerlies](/wiki/Westerlies "Westerlies").[[17]](#cite_note-17) Cyclone formation is usually reduced 3 days prior to the wave's crest and increased during the 3 days after.[[18]](#cite_note-18)

### Formation regions and warning centers

Main articles: [Tropical cyclone basins](/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_basins "Tropical cyclone basins") and [Regional Specialized Meteorological Centre](/wiki/Regional_Specialized_Meteorological_Centre "Regional Specialized Meteorological Centre")

Tropical cyclone basins and official warning centers

| Basin | Warning center | Area of responsibility | Notes |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| **Northern Hemisphere** | | | |
| [North Atlantic](/wiki/Atlantic_hurricane "Atlantic hurricane") | United States [National Hurricane Center](/wiki/National_Hurricane_Center "National Hurricane Center") (Miami) | Equator northward, African Coast – 140°W | [[19]](#cite_note-RA_IV_HOP-19) |
| [Eastern Pacific](/wiki/Pacific_hurricane "Pacific hurricane") | United States [Central Pacific Hurricane Center](/wiki/Central_Pacific_Hurricane_Center "Central Pacific Hurricane Center") (Honolulu) | Equator northward, 140–180°W | [[19]](#cite_note-RA_IV_HOP-19) |
| [Western Pacific](/wiki/Typhoon "Typhoon") | [Japan Meteorological Agency](/wiki/Japan_Meteorological_Agency "Japan Meteorological Agency") | Equator – 60°N, 180–100°E | [[20]](#cite_note-WPAC_TCOP-20) |
| [North Indian Ocean](/wiki/North_Indian_Ocean_tropical_cyclone "North Indian Ocean tropical cyclone") | [India Meteorological Department](/wiki/India_Meteorological_Department "India Meteorological Department") | Equator northwards, 100–40°E | [[21]](#cite_note-NIO_TCOP-21) |
| **Southern Hemisphere** | | | |
| [South-West Indian Ocean](/wiki/South-West_Indian_Ocean_tropical_cyclone "South-West Indian Ocean tropical cyclone") | [Météo-France](/wiki/M%C3%A9t%C3%A9o-France "Météo-France") Reunion | Equator – 40°S, African Coast – 90°E | [[22]](#cite_note-SWIO_TCOP-22) |
| [Australian region](/wiki/Australian_region_tropical_cyclone "Australian region tropical cyclone") | Indonesian [Meteorology, Climatology,  and Geophysical Agency](/wiki/Meteorology,_Climatology,_and_Geophysical_Agency "Meteorology, Climatology, and Geophysical Agency") (BMKG) | Equator – 10°S, 90–141°E | [[23]](#cite_note-SPAC_TCOP-23) |
| Papua New Guinea National Weather Service | Equator – 10°S, 141–160°E | [[23]](#cite_note-SPAC_TCOP-23) |
| Australian [Bureau of Meteorology](/wiki/Bureau_of_Meteorology "Bureau of Meteorology") | 10–40°S, 90–160°E | [[23]](#cite_note-SPAC_TCOP-23) |
| [Southern Pacific](/wiki/South_Pacific_tropical_cyclone "South Pacific tropical cyclone") | [Fiji Meteorological Service](/wiki/Fiji_Meteorological_Service "Fiji Meteorological Service") | Equator – 25°S, 160°E – 120°W | [[23]](#cite_note-SPAC_TCOP-23) |
| [Meteorological Service of New Zealand](/wiki/Meteorological_Service_of_New_Zealand "Meteorological Service of New Zealand") | 25–40°S, 160°E – 120°W | [[23]](#cite_note-SPAC_TCOP-23) |

The majority of tropical cyclones each year form in one of seven tropical cyclone basins, which are monitored by a variety of meteorological services and warning centers.[[1]](#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-1) Ten of these warning centers worldwide are designated as either a [Regional Specialized Meteorological Centre](/wiki/Regional_Specialized_Meteorological_Centre "Regional Specialized Meteorological Centre") or a [Tropical Cyclone Warning Centre](/wiki/Regional_Specialized_Meteorological_Centre#Tropical_cyclones "Regional Specialized Meteorological Centre") by the [World Meteorological Organization](/wiki/World_Meteorological_Organization "World Meteorological Organization")'s (WMO) tropical cyclone programme.[[1]](#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-1) These warning centers issue advisories which provide basic information and cover a systems present, forecast position, movement and intensity, in their designated areas of responsibility.[[1]](#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-1)

Meteorological services around the world are generally responsible for issuing warnings for their own country. There are exceptions, as the United States National Hurricane Center and Fiji Meteorological Service issue alerts, watches and warnings for various island nations in their areas of responsibility.[[1]](#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-1)[[23]](#cite_note-SPAC_TCOP-23) The United States [Joint Typhoon Warning Center](/wiki/Joint_Typhoon_Warning_Center "Joint Typhoon Warning Center") and Fleet Weather Center also publicly issue warnings about tropical cyclones on behalf of the [United States Government](/wiki/United_States_Government "United States Government").[[1]](#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-1) The [Brazilian Navy](/wiki/Brazilian_Navy "Brazilian Navy") Hydrographic Center names [South Atlantic tropical cyclones](/wiki/South_Atlantic_tropical_cyclone "South Atlantic tropical cyclone"), however the South Atlantic is not a major basin, and not an official basin according to the WMO.[[24]](#cite_note-names_2011-24)

## Interactions with climate

Main article: [Tropical cyclones by year](/wiki/Tropical_cyclones_by_year "Tropical cyclones by year")

Each year on average, around 80 to 90 named tropical cyclones form around the world, of which over half develop hurricane-force winds of 65 kn (120 km/h; 75 mph) or more.[[1]](#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-1) Worldwide, tropical cyclone activity peaks in late summer, when the difference between temperatures aloft and [sea surface temperatures](/wiki/Sea_surface_temperature "Sea surface temperature") is the greatest. However, each particular basin has its own seasonal patterns. On a worldwide scale, May is the least active month, while September is the most active month. November is the only month in which all the [tropical cyclone basins](/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_basins "Tropical cyclone basins") are in season.[[25]](#cite_note-AOML_FAQ_G1-25)

In the Northern [Atlantic Ocean](/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean "Atlantic Ocean"), a distinct [cyclone season](/wiki/Atlantic_hurricane_season "Atlantic hurricane season") occurs from June 1 to November 30, sharply peaking from late August through September.[[25]](#cite_note-AOML_FAQ_G1-25) The statistical peak of the Atlantic hurricane season is September 10.[[26]](#cite_note-NHC_Atl_climatology-26)

The Northeast Pacific Ocean has a broader period of activity, but in a similar time frame to the Atlantic.[[26]](#cite_note-NHC_Atl_climatology-26) The Northwest Pacific sees tropical cyclones year-round, with a minimum in February and March and a peak in early September.[[25]](#cite_note-AOML_FAQ_G1-25) In the North Indian basin, storms are most common from April to December, with peaks in May and November.[[25]](#cite_note-AOML_FAQ_G1-25) In the Southern Hemisphere, the tropical cyclone year begins on July 1 and runs all year-round encompassing the tropical cyclone seasons, which run from November 1 until the end of April, with peaks in mid-February to early March.[[25]](#cite_note-AOML_FAQ_G1-25)[[23]](#cite_note-SPAC_TCOP-23)

Of various [modes of variability](/wiki/Climate_variability_and_change "Climate variability and change") in the climate system, [El Niño–Southern Oscillation](/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o%E2%80%93Southern_Oscillation "El Niño–Southern Oscillation") has the largest effect on tropical cyclone activity.[[27]](#cite_note-:3-27) Most tropical cyclones form on the side of the subtropical ridge closer to the equator, then move poleward past the ridge axis before recurving into the main belt of the [Westerlies](/wiki/Westerlies "Westerlies").[[28]](#cite_note-28) When the [subtropical ridge](/wiki/Subtropical_ridge "Subtropical ridge") position shifts due to El Niño, so will the preferred tropical cyclone tracks. Areas west of Japan and [Korea](/wiki/Korea "Korea") tend to experience much fewer September–November tropical cyclone impacts during [El Niño](/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o "El Niño") and neutral years.[[29]](#cite_note-China-29)

During [La Niña](/wiki/La_Ni%C3%B1a "La Niña") years, the formation of tropical cyclones, along with the subtropical ridge position, shifts westward across the western Pacific Ocean, which increases the landfall threat to China and much greater intensity in the [Philippines](/wiki/Philippines "Philippines").[[29]](#cite_note-China-29) The Atlantic Ocean experiences depressed activity due to increased vertical [wind shear](/wiki/Wind_shear "Wind shear") across the region during El Niño years.[[30]](#cite_note-30) Tropical cyclones are further influenced by the [Atlantic Meridional Mode](/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Meridional_Mode&action=edit&redlink=1 "Atlantic Meridional Mode (page does not exist)"), the [Quasi-biennial oscillation](/wiki/Quasi-biennial_oscillation "Quasi-biennial oscillation") and the [Madden–Julian oscillation](/wiki/Madden%E2%80%93Julian_oscillation "Madden–Julian oscillation").[[27]](#cite_note-:3-27)[[31]](#cite_note-31)

Season lengths and averages

| Basin | Season start | Season end | Tropical cyclones | Refs |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
| North Atlantic | June 1 | November 30 | 14.4 | [[32]](#cite_note-AOML_FAQ_E10-32) |
| Eastern Pacific | May 15 | November 30 | 16.6 | [[32]](#cite_note-AOML_FAQ_E10-32) |
| Western Pacific | January 1 | December 31 | 26.0 | [[32]](#cite_note-AOML_FAQ_E10-32) |
| North Indian | January 1 | December 31 | 12 | [[33]](#cite_note-33) |
| South-West Indian | July 1 | June 30 | 9.3 | [[32]](#cite_note-AOML_FAQ_E10-32)[[22]](#cite_note-SWIO_TCOP-22) |
| Australian region | November 1 | April 30 | 11.0 | [[34]](#cite_note-Aus_TC_Outlook-34) |
| Southern Pacific | November 1 | April 30 | 7.1 | [[35]](#cite_note-2019-20_SO-35) |
| Total: | | | 96.4 | |

### Influence of climate change

Main article: [Tropical cyclones and climate change](/wiki/Tropical_cyclones_and_climate_change "Tropical cyclones and climate change")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/1980-_Atlantic_region_category_4_and_5_hurricanes_-_NYTimes_and_NOAA.svg/250px-1980-_Atlantic_region_category_4_and_5_hurricanes_-_NYTimes_and_NOAA.svg.png)](/wiki/File:1980-_Atlantic_region_category_4_and_5_hurricanes_-_NYTimes_and_NOAA.svg)

The 20-year average of the number of annual Category 4 and 5 hurricanes in the Atlantic region has approximately doubled since the year 2000.[[36]](#cite_note-NYTimes_20220929-36)

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/1920-_Category_5_Atlantic_hurricanes.svg/250px-1920-_Category_5_Atlantic_hurricanes.svg.png)](/wiki/File:1920-_Category_5_Atlantic_hurricanes.svg)

Category 5 hurricanes have become much more common in recent decades.[[37]](#cite_note-USAtoday_202511-37)

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/2024_Climate_change_increasing_Atlantic_hurricane_peak_wind_speeds.svg/330px-2024_Climate_change_increasing_Atlantic_hurricane_peak_wind_speeds.svg.png)](/wiki/File:2024_Climate_change_increasing_Atlantic_hurricane_peak_wind_speeds.svg)

Climate change's increase of water temperatures intensified peak wind speeds in all eleven 2024 Atlantic hurricanes.[[38]](#cite_note-EnvRschClimate_20241120-38)

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/202307_Survey_-_climate_change_%22major_factor%22_in_extreme_weather_-_Washington_Post.svg/330px-202307_Survey_-_climate_change_%22major_factor%22_in_extreme_weather_-_Washington_Post.svg.png)](/wiki/File:202307_Survey_-_climate_change_%22major_factor%22_in_extreme_weather_-_Washington_Post.svg)

Perceptions in the [United States](/wiki/United_States "United States") differ along political lines, on whether climate change was a "major factor" contributing to various extreme weather events experienced by respondents in 2023.[[39]](#cite_note-WashPost_20230823-39) "Severe storms" includes hurricanes.

The [IPCC Sixth Assessment Report](/wiki/IPCC_Sixth_Assessment_Report "IPCC Sixth Assessment Report") summarize the latest scientific findings about the impact of climate change on tropical cyclones. According to the report, we have now better understanding about the impact of climate change on tropical storm than before. Major tropical storms *likely* became more frequent in the last 40 years. We can say with *high confidence* that climate change increased rainfall during tropical cyclones. We can say with *high confidence* that a 1.5 degree warming lead to "increased proportion of and peak wind speeds of intense tropical cyclones". We can say with *medium confidence* that regional impacts of further warming include more intense tropical cyclones and/or extratropical storms.[[40]](#cite_note-40)

[Climate change](/wiki/Climate_change "Climate change") can affect tropical cyclones in a variety of ways: an intensification of rainfall and wind speed, a decrease in overall frequency, an increase in the frequency of very intense storms and a poleward extension of where the cyclones reach maximum intensity are among the possible consequences of human-induced climate change.[[2]](#cite_note-:5-2) Tropical cyclones use warm, moist air as their fuel. As climate change is [warming ocean temperatures](/wiki/Ocean_heat_content "Ocean heat content"), there is potentially more of this fuel available.[[3]](#cite_note-auto-3)

Between 1979 and 2017, there was a global increase in the proportion of tropical cyclones of Category 3 and higher on the [Saffir–Simpson scale](/wiki/Saffir%E2%80%93Simpson_scale "Saffir–Simpson scale"). The trend was most clear in the North Atlantic and in the Southern Indian Ocean. In the North Pacific, tropical cyclones have been moving poleward into colder waters and there was no increase in intensity over this period.[[41]](#cite_note-41) With 2 °C (3.6 °F) warming, a greater percentage (+13%) of tropical cyclones are expected to reach Category 4 and 5 strength.[[2]](#cite_note-:5-2) A 2019 study indicates that climate change has been driving the observed trend of [rapid intensification](/wiki/Rapid_intensification "Rapid intensification") of tropical cyclones in the Atlantic basin. Rapidly intensifying cyclones are hard to forecast and therefore pose additional risk to coastal communities.[[42]](#cite_note-42)

Warmer air can hold more water vapor: the theoretical maximum water vapor content is given by the [Clausius–Clapeyron relation](/wiki/Clausius%E2%80%93Clapeyron_relation "Clausius–Clapeyron relation"), which yields ≈7% increase in water vapor in the atmosphere per 1 °C (1.8 °F) warming.[[43]](#cite_note-43)[[44]](#cite_note-knutson_2013-44) All models that were assessed in a 2019 review paper show a future increase of rainfall rates.[[2]](#cite_note-:5-2) Additional [sea level rise](/wiki/Sea_level_rise "Sea level rise") will increase storm surge levels.[[45]](#cite_note-:6-45)[[46]](#cite_note-:4-46) It is plausible that extreme [wind waves](/wiki/Wind_wave "Wind wave") see an increase as a consequence of changes in tropical cyclones, further exacerbating storm surge dangers to coastal communities.[[47]](#cite_note-:7-47) The compounding effects from floods, storm surge, and terrestrial flooding (rivers) are projected to increase [due to global warming](/wiki/Effects_of_global_warming "Effects of global warming").[[46]](#cite_note-:4-46)

There is currently no consensus on how climate change will affect the overall frequency of tropical cyclones.[[2]](#cite_note-:5-2) A majority of [climate models](/wiki/Climate_model "Climate model") show a decreased frequency in future projections.[[47]](#cite_note-:7-47) For instance, a 2020 paper comparing nine high-resolution climate models found robust decreases in frequency in the Southern Indian Ocean and the Southern Hemisphere more generally, while finding mixed signals for Northern Hemisphere tropical cyclones.[[48]](#cite_note-48) Observations have shown little change in the overall frequency of tropical cyclones worldwide,[[49]](#cite_note-49) with increased frequency in the North Atlantic and central Pacific, and significant decreases in the southern Indian Ocean and western North Pacific.[[50]](#cite_note-50)

There has been a poleward expansion of the latitude at which the maximum intensity of tropical cyclones occurs, which may be associated with climate change.[[51]](#cite_note-51) In the North Pacific, there may also have been an eastward expansion.[[45]](#cite_note-:6-45) Between 1949 and 2016, there was a slowdown in tropical cyclone translation speeds. (Tropical cyclone translation speed is the speed at which a storm moves across the ocean, measured at consecutive locations at a selected time interval, such as every three hours or every six hours.) It is unclear still to what extent this can be attributed to climate change: climate models do not all show this feature.[[47]](#cite_note-:7-47)

A 2021 study review article concluded that the geographic range of tropical cyclones will probably expand poleward in response to climate warming of the [Hadley circulation](/wiki/Hadley_circulation "Hadley circulation").[[52]](#cite_note-52)

When hurricane winds speed rise by 5%, its destructive power rises by about 50%. Therefore, as climate change increased the wind speed of [Hurricane Helene](/wiki/Hurricane_Helene "Hurricane Helene") by 11%, it increased the destruction from it by more than twice.[[53]](#cite_note-53) According to [World Weather Attribution](/wiki/World_Weather_Attribution "World Weather Attribution") the influence of climate change on the rainfall of some latest hurricanes can be described as follows:[[54]](#cite_note-54)

Impact of climate change on the rainfall during some recent hurricanes according to [World Weather Attribution](/wiki/World_Weather_Attribution "World Weather Attribution").

| The name of the hurricane | How much climate change increased rainfall |
| --- | --- |
| [Hurricane Katrina](/wiki/Hurricane_Katrina "Hurricane Katrina") | 4% |
| [Hurricane Irma](/wiki/Hurricane_Irma "Hurricane Irma") | 6% |
| [Hurricane Maria](/wiki/Hurricane_Maria "Hurricane Maria") | 9% |
| [Hurricane Florence](/wiki/Hurricane_Florence "Hurricane Florence") | 5% |
| [Hurricane Dorian](/wiki/Hurricane_Dorian "Hurricane Dorian") | 5–18% |
| [Hurricane Ian](/wiki/Hurricane_Ian "Hurricane Ian") | 18% |
| [Hurricane Harvey](/wiki/Hurricane_Harvey "Hurricane Harvey") | 7–38% |
| [Hurricane Helene](/wiki/Hurricane_Helene "Hurricane Helene") | 10% |

## Intensity

Tropical cyclone intensity is based on wind speeds and pressure. Relationships between winds and pressure are often used in determining the intensity of a storm.[[55]](#cite_note-55) [Tropical cyclone scales](/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_scales "Tropical cyclone scales"), such as the [Saffir-Simpson hurricane wind scale](/wiki/Saffir-Simpson_hurricane_wind_scale "Saffir-Simpson hurricane wind scale") and Australia's scale (Bureau of Meteorology), only use wind speed for determining the category of a storm.[[56]](#cite_note-56)[[57]](#cite_note-57) The most intense storm on record is [Typhoon Tip](/wiki/Typhoon_Tip "Typhoon Tip") in the northwestern Pacific Ocean in 1979, which reached a minimum pressure of 870 [hPa](/wiki/Pascal_(unit) "Pascal (unit)") (26 [inHg](/wiki/Inch_of_mercury "Inch of mercury")) and maximum sustained wind speeds of 165 kn (85 m/s; 305 km/h; 190 mph).[[58]](#cite_note-jtwc-58) The highest maximum sustained wind speed ever recorded was 185 kn (95 m/s; 345 km/h; 215 mph) in [Hurricane Patricia](/wiki/Hurricane_Patricia "Hurricane Patricia") in 2015—the most intense cyclone ever recorded in the [Western Hemisphere](/wiki/Western_Hemisphere "Western Hemisphere").[[59]](#cite_note-59)

### Factors

Warm [sea surface temperatures](/wiki/Sea_surface_temperature "Sea surface temperature") are required for tropical cyclones to form and strengthen. The commonly accepted minimum temperature range for this to occur is 26–27 °C (79–81 °F), however, multiple studies have proposed a lower minimum of 25.5 °C (77.9 °F).[[60]](#cite_note-60)[[61]](#cite_note-61) Higher sea surface temperatures result in faster intensification rates and sometimes even [rapid intensification](/wiki/Rapid_intensification "Rapid intensification").[[62]](#cite_note-62) High [ocean heat content](/wiki/Ocean_heat_content "Ocean heat content"), also known as [Tropical Cyclone Heat Potential](/wiki/Tropical_Cyclone_Heat_Potential "Tropical Cyclone Heat Potential"), allows storms to achieve a higher intensity.[[63]](#cite_note-NHC_Intensity_Forecasting-63) Most tropical cyclones that experience rapid intensification are traversing regions of high ocean heat content rather than lower values.[[64]](#cite_note-OHC-64) High ocean heat content values can help to offset the oceanic cooling caused by the passage of a tropical cyclone, limiting the effect this cooling has on the storm.[[65]](#cite_note-65) Faster-moving systems are able to intensify to higher intensities with lower ocean heat content values. Slower-moving systems require higher values of ocean heat content to achieve the same intensity.[[64]](#cite_note-OHC-64)

The passage of a tropical cyclone over the ocean causes the upper layers of the ocean to cool substantially, a process known as [upwelling](/wiki/Upwelling "Upwelling"),[[66]](#cite_note-66) which can negatively influence subsequent cyclone development. This cooling is primarily caused by wind-driven mixing of cold water from deeper in the ocean with the warm surface waters. This effect results in a negative feedback process that can inhibit further development or lead to weakening. Additional cooling may come in the form of cold water from falling raindrops (this is because the atmosphere is cooler at higher altitudes). Cloud cover may also play a role in cooling the ocean, by shielding the ocean surface from direct sunlight before and slightly after the storm passage. All these effects can combine to produce a dramatic drop in sea surface temperature over a large area in just a few days.[[67]](#cite_note-NASA_Cooling-67) Conversely, the mixing of the sea can result in heat being inserted in deeper waters, with [potential effects](/wiki/Cyclonic_Ni%C3%B1o "Cyclonic Niño") on global [climate](/wiki/Climate "Climate").[[68]](#cite_note-68)

[Vertical wind shear](/wiki/Vertical_wind_shear "Vertical wind shear") decreases tropical cyclone predicability, with storms exhibiting wide range of responses in the presence of shear.[[69]](#cite_note-69) Wind shear often negatively affects tropical cyclone intensification by displacing moisture and heat from a system's center.[[70]](#cite_note-70) Low levels of vertical wind shear are most optimal for strengthening, while stronger wind shear induces weakening.[[71]](#cite_note-71)[[72]](#cite_note-72) Dry air entraining into a tropical cyclone's core has a negative effect on its development and intensity by diminishing atmospheric convection and introducing asymmetries in the storm's structure.[[73]](#cite_note-73)[[74]](#cite_note-74)[[75]](#cite_note-75) Symmetric, strong [outflow](/wiki/Outflow_(meteorology) "Outflow (meteorology)") leads to a faster rate of intensification than observed in other systems by mitigating local wind shear.[[76]](#cite_note-76)[[77]](#cite_note-77)[[78]](#cite_note-78) Weakening outflow is associated with the weakening of [rainbands](/wiki/Rainband "Rainband") within a tropical cyclone.[[79]](#cite_note-79) Tropical cyclones may still intensify, even rapidly, in the presence of moderate or strong wind shear depending on the evolution and structure of the storm's convection.[[80]](#cite_note-80)[[81]](#cite_note-81)

The size of tropical cyclones plays a role in how quickly they intensify. Smaller tropical cyclones are more prone to rapid intensification than larger ones.[[82]](#cite_note-82) The [Fujiwhara effect](/wiki/Fujiwhara_effect "Fujiwhara effect"), which involves interaction between two tropical cyclones, can weaken and ultimately result in the dissipation of the weaker of two tropical cyclones by reducing the organization of the system's convection and imparting horizontal wind shear.[[83]](#cite_note-83) Tropical cyclones typically weaken while situated over a landmass because conditions are often unfavorable as a result of the lack of oceanic forcing.[[84]](#cite_note-84) The [Brown ocean effect](/wiki/Brown_ocean_effect "Brown ocean effect") can allow a tropical cyclone to maintain or increase its intensity following [landfall](/wiki/Landfall "Landfall"), in cases where there has been copious rainfall, through the release of latent heat from the saturated soil.[[85]](#cite_note-85) [Orographic lift](/wiki/Orographic_lift "Orographic lift") can cause a significant increase in the intensity of the convection of a tropical cyclone when its eye moves over a mountain, breaking the capped boundary layer that had been restraining it.[[86]](#cite_note-86) Jet streams can both enhance and inhibit tropical cyclone intensity by influencing the storm's outflow as well as vertical wind shear.[[87]](#cite_note-87)[[88]](#cite_note-88)

### Rapid intensification

Main article: [Rapid intensification](/wiki/Rapid_intensification "Rapid intensification")

On occasion, tropical cyclones may undergo a process known as rapid intensification, a period in which the maximum sustained winds of a tropical cyclone increase by 30 [kn](/wiki/Knot_(unit) "Knot (unit)") (56 km/h; 35 mph) or more within 24 hours.[[89]](#cite_note-NWS_Glossary-89) Similarly, rapid deepening in tropical cyclones is defined as a minimum sea surface pressure decrease of 1.75 hPa (0.052 inHg) per hour or 42 hPa (1.2 inHg) within a 24-hour period; explosive deepening occurs when the surface pressure decreases by 2.5 hPa (0.074 inHg) per hour for at least 12 hours or 5 hPa (0.15 inHg) per hour for at least 6 hours.[[90]](#cite_note-90)

For rapid intensification to occur, several conditions must be in place. Water temperatures must be extremely high, near or above 30 °C (86 °F), and water of this temperature must be sufficiently deep such that waves do not upwell cooler waters to the surface. On the other hand, [Tropical Cyclone Heat Potential](/wiki/Tropical_Cyclone_Heat_Potential "Tropical Cyclone Heat Potential") is one of such non-conventional subsurface [oceanographic](/wiki/Oceanographic "Oceanographic") parameters influencing the [cyclone](/wiki/Cyclone "Cyclone") intensity.[[91]](#cite_note-Energetics-91)

[Wind shear](/wiki/Wind_shear "Wind shear") must be low. When wind shear is high, the [convection](/wiki/Convection#Atmospheric_convection "Convection") and circulation in the cyclone will be disrupted. Usually, an [anticyclone](/wiki/Anticyclone "Anticyclone") in the upper layers of the [troposphere](/wiki/Troposphere "Troposphere") above the storm must be present as well—for extremely low surface pressures to develop, air must be rising very rapidly in the eyewall of the storm, and an upper-level anticyclone helps channel this air away from the cyclone efficiently.[[91]](#cite_note-Energetics-91) However, some cyclones such as [Hurricane Epsilon](/wiki/Hurricane_Epsilon_(2020) "Hurricane Epsilon (2020)") have rapidly intensified despite relatively unfavorable conditions.[[92]](#cite_note-92)[[93]](#cite_note-93)

### Dissipation

[![Satellite image of a cyclone where the thickest clouds are displaced from the central vortex](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Paulette_2020-09-10_1620Z.jpg/250px-Paulette_2020-09-10_1620Z.jpg)](/wiki/File:Paulette_2020-09-10_1620Z.jpg)

[Hurricane Paulette](/wiki/Hurricane_Paulette_(2020) "Hurricane Paulette (2020)"), in [2020](/wiki/2020_Atlantic_hurricane_season "2020 Atlantic hurricane season"), is an example of a [sheared](/wiki/Windshear "Windshear") tropical cyclone, with deep [convection](/wiki/Atmospheric_convection "Atmospheric convection") slightly removed from the center of the system.

There are a number of ways a tropical cyclone can weaken, dissipate, or lose its tropical characteristics. These include making landfall, moving over cooler water, encountering dry air, or interacting with other weather systems; however, once a system has dissipated or lost its tropical characteristics, its remnants could regenerate a tropical cyclone if environmental conditions become favorable.[[94]](#cite_note-HurricaneGraveyard-94)[[95]](#cite_note-Sadler_and_Kilonsky_1977-95)

A tropical cyclone can dissipate when it moves over waters significantly cooler than 26.5 °C (79.7 °F). This will deprive the storm of such tropical characteristics as a warm core with thunderstorms near the center, so that it becomes a remnant [low-pressure area](/wiki/Low-pressure_area "Low-pressure area"). Remnant systems may persist for several days before losing their identity. This dissipation mechanism is most common in the eastern North Pacific. Weakening or dissipation can also occur if a storm experiences vertical wind shear which causes the convection and heat engine to move away from the center. This normally ceases the development of a tropical cyclone.[[96]](#cite_note-EAM-96) In addition, its interaction with the main belt of the [Westerlies](/wiki/Westerlies "Westerlies"), by means of merging with a nearby frontal zone, can cause tropical cyclones to evolve into [extratropical cyclones](/wiki/Extratropical_cyclones "Extratropical cyclones"). This transition can take 1–3 days.[[97]](#cite_note-JWTC_intensity-97)

Should a tropical cyclone make landfall or pass over an island, its circulation could start to break down, especially if it encounters mountainous terrain.[[98]](#cite_note-LifeCycle-98) When a system makes landfall on a large landmass, it is cut off from its supply of warm moist maritime air and starts to draw in dry continental air.[[98]](#cite_note-LifeCycle-98) This, combined with the increased friction over land areas, leads to the weakening and dissipation of the tropical cyclone.[[98]](#cite_note-LifeCycle-98) Over a mountainous terrain, a system can quickly weaken. Over flat areas, it may endure for two to three days before circulation breaks down and dissipates.[[98]](#cite_note-LifeCycle-98)

Over the years, there have been a number of techniques considered to try to artificially modify tropical cyclones.[[99]](#cite_note-Stop-99) These techniques have included using [nuclear weapons](/wiki/Nuclear_weapon "Nuclear weapon"), cooling the ocean with icebergs, blowing the storm away from land with giant fans, and [seeding](/wiki/Cloud_seeding "Cloud seeding") selected storms with dry ice or [silver iodide](/wiki/Silver_iodide "Silver iodide").[[99]](#cite_note-Stop-99) These techniques, however, fail to appreciate the duration, intensity, power or size of tropical cyclones.[[99]](#cite_note-Stop-99)

### Assessment methods

For broader coverage of this topic, see [Dvorak technique](/wiki/Dvorak_technique "Dvorak technique") and [Scatterometer](/wiki/Scatterometer "Scatterometer").

A variety of methods or techniques, including surface, satellite, and aerial, are used to assess the intensity of a tropical cyclone. Reconnaissance aircraft fly around and through tropical cyclones, outfitted with specialized instruments, to collect information that can be used to ascertain the winds and pressure of a system.[[1]](#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-1) Tropical cyclones possess winds of different speeds at different heights. Winds recorded at flight level can be converted to find the wind speeds at the surface.[[100]](#cite_note-100) Surface observations, such as ship reports, land stations, [mesonets](/wiki/Mesonet "Mesonet"), coastal stations, and buoys, can provide information on a tropical cyclone's intensity or the direction it is traveling.[[1]](#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-1)

Wind-pressure relationships (WPRs) are used as a way to determine the pressure of a storm based on its wind speed. Several different methods and equations have been proposed to calculate WPRs.[[101]](#cite_note-101)[[102]](#cite_note-WPRs-102) Tropical cyclones agencies each use their own, fixed WPR, which can result in inaccuracies between agencies that are issuing estimates on the same system.[[102]](#cite_note-WPRs-102) The ASCAT is a [scatterometer](/wiki/Scatterometer "Scatterometer") used by the [MetOp](/wiki/MetOp "MetOp") satellites to map the wind field vectors of tropical cyclones.[[1]](#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-1) The SMAP uses an L-band [radiometer](/wiki/Radiometer "Radiometer") channel to determine the wind speeds of tropical cyclones at the ocean surface, and has been shown to be reliable at higher intensities and under heavy rainfall conditions, unlike scatterometer-based and other radiometer-based instruments.[[103]](#cite_note-103)

The [Dvorak technique](/wiki/Dvorak_technique "Dvorak technique") plays a large role in both the classification of a tropical cyclone and the determination of its intensity. Used in warning centers, the method was developed by [Vernon Dvorak](/wiki/Vernon_Dvorak "Vernon Dvorak") in the 1970s, and uses both visible and infrared satellite imagery in the assessment of tropical cyclone intensity. The Dvorak technique uses a scale of "T-numbers", scaling in increments of 0.5 from T1.0 to T8.0. Each T-number has an intensity assigned to it, with larger T-numbers indicating a stronger system. Tropical cyclones are assessed by forecasters according to an array of patterns, including [curved banding features](/wiki/Rainband "Rainband"), shear, central dense overcast, and eye, to determine the T-number and thus assess the intensity of the storm.[[104]](#cite_note-104)

The [Cooperative Institute for Meteorological Satellite Studies](/wiki/Cooperative_Institute_for_Meteorological_Satellite_Studies "Cooperative Institute for Meteorological Satellite Studies") works to develop and improve automated satellite methods, such as the Advanced Dvorak Technique (ADT) and SATCON. The ADT, used by a large number of forecasting centers, uses infrared geostationary satellite imagery and an algorithm based upon the Dvorak technique to assess the intensity of tropical cyclones. The ADT has a number of differences from the conventional Dvorak technique, including changes to intensity constraint rules and the usage of microwave imagery to base a system's intensity upon its internal structure, which prevents the intensity from leveling off before an eye emerges in infrared imagery.[[105]](#cite_note-105) The SATCON weights estimates from various satellite-based systems and [microwave sounders](/wiki/Advanced_microwave_sounding_unit "Advanced microwave sounding unit"), accounting for the strengths and flaws in each individual estimate, to produce a consensus estimate of a tropical cyclone's intensity which can be more reliable than the Dvorak technique at times.[[106]](#cite_note-106)[[107]](#cite_note-107)

### Intensity metrics

Multiple intensity metrics are used, including [accumulated cyclone energy](/wiki/Accumulated_cyclone_energy "Accumulated cyclone energy") (ACE), the [Hurricane Surge Index](/w/index.php?title=Hurricane_Surge_Index&action=edit&redlink=1 "Hurricane Surge Index (page does not exist)"), the [Hurricane Severity Index](/wiki/Hurricane_Severity_Index "Hurricane Severity Index"), the [Power Dissipation Index](/w/index.php?title=Power_Dissipation_Index&action=edit&redlink=1 "Power Dissipation Index (page does not exist)") (PDI), and [integrated kinetic energy](/w/index.php?title=Integrated_kinetic_energy&action=edit&redlink=1 "Integrated kinetic energy (page does not exist)") (IKE). ACE is a metric of the total energy a system has exerted over its lifespan. ACE is calculated by summing the squares of a cyclone's sustained wind speed, every six hours as long as the system is at or above tropical storm intensity and either tropical or subtropical.[[108]](#cite_note-108) The calculation of the PDI is similar in nature to ACE, with the major difference being that wind speeds are cubed rather than squared.[[109]](#cite_note-109)

The Hurricane Surge Index is a metric of the potential damage a storm may inflict via storm surge. It is calculated by squaring the dividend of the storm's wind speed and a climatological value (33 m/s or 74 mph), and then multiplying that quantity by the dividend of the radius of hurricane-force winds and its climatological value (96.6 km or 60.0 mi). This can be represented in equation form as:
