## Classification and naming

### Classification

Main article: [Tropical cyclone scales](/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_scales "Tropical cyclone scales")

[![Satellite image of three simultaneous tropical cyclones](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Maria%2C_Bopha_and_Saomai_2006-08-07_0435Z.jpg/250px-Maria%2C_Bopha_and_Saomai_2006-08-07_0435Z.jpg)](/wiki/File:Maria,_Bopha_and_Saomai_2006-08-07_0435Z.jpg)

Three tropical cyclones of the [2006 Pacific typhoon season](/wiki/2006_Pacific_typhoon_season "2006 Pacific typhoon season") at different stages of development. [The weakest](/wiki/2006_Pacific_typhoon_season#Severe_Tropical_Storm_Bopha_(Inday) "2006 Pacific typhoon season") (left) demonstrates only the most basic circular shape. [A stronger storm](/wiki/Typhoon_Maria_(2006) "Typhoon Maria (2006)") (top right) demonstrates [spiral banding](/wiki/Rainband "Rainband") and increased centralization, while the [strongest](/wiki/Typhoon_Saomai "Typhoon Saomai") (lower right) has developed an [eye](/wiki/Eye_(cyclone) "Eye (cyclone)").

Around the world, tropical cyclones are classified in different ways, based on the location ([tropical cyclone basins](/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_basins "Tropical cyclone basins")), the structure of the system and its intensity. For example, within the Northern Atlantic and Eastern Pacific basins, a tropical cyclone with wind speeds of over 65 [kn](/wiki/Knot_(unit) "Knot (unit)") (120 km/h; 75 mph) is called a **hurricane**, while it is called a **typhoon** or a severe cyclonic storm within the Western Pacific or North Indian oceans.[[19]](#cite_note-RA_IV_HOP-19)[[20]](#cite_note-WPAC_TCOP-20)[[21]](#cite_note-NIO_TCOP-21) When a hurricane passes west across the [International Dateline](/wiki/International_Dateline "International Dateline") in the Northern Hemisphere, it becomes known as a typhoon. This happened in 2014 for [Hurricane Genevieve](/wiki/Hurricane_Genevieve_(2014) "Hurricane Genevieve (2014)"), which became Typhoon Genevieve.[[113]](#cite_note-113)

Within the Southern Hemisphere, it is either called a hurricane, tropical cyclone or a severe tropical cyclone, depending on if it is located within the South Atlantic, South-West Indian Ocean, Australian region or the South Pacific Ocean.[[22]](#cite_note-SWIO_TCOP-22)[[23]](#cite_note-SPAC_TCOP-23) The descriptors for tropical cyclones with wind speeds below 65 kn (120 km/h; 75 mph) vary by tropical cyclone basin and may be further subdivided into categories such as "tropical storm", "cyclonic storm", "tropical depression", or "deep depression".[[20]](#cite_note-WPAC_TCOP-20)[[21]](#cite_note-NIO_TCOP-21)[[19]](#cite_note-RA_IV_HOP-19)

### Naming

Main articles: [Tropical cyclone naming](/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_naming "Tropical cyclone naming") and [History of tropical cyclone naming](/wiki/History_of_tropical_cyclone_naming "History of tropical cyclone naming")

The practice of using [given names](/wiki/Given_name "Given name") to identify tropical cyclones dates back to the late 1800s and early 1900s and gradually superseded the existing system—simply naming cyclones based on what they hit.[[114]](#cite_note-Whats_in_a_name?-114)[[115]](#cite_note-Mahina-115) The system currently used provides positive identification of severe weather systems in a brief form, that is readily understood and recognized by the public.[[114]](#cite_note-Whats_in_a_name?-114)[[115]](#cite_note-Mahina-115) The credit for the first usage of personal names for weather systems is generally given to the [Queensland Government](/wiki/Queensland_Government "Queensland Government") Meteorologist [Clement Wragge](/wiki/Clement_Wragge "Clement Wragge") who named systems between 1887 and 1907.[[114]](#cite_note-Whats_in_a_name?-114)[[115]](#cite_note-Mahina-115) This system of naming weather systems fell into disuse for several years after Wragge retired, until it was revived in the latter part of [World War II](/wiki/World_War_II "World War II") for the Western Pacific.[[114]](#cite_note-Whats_in_a_name?-114)[[115]](#cite_note-Mahina-115) Formal naming schemes have subsequently been introduced for the North and South Atlantic, Eastern, Central, Western and Southern Pacific basins as well as the Australian region and Indian Ocean.[[115]](#cite_note-Mahina-115)

At present, tropical cyclones are officially named by one of twelve [meteorological services](/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_naming "Tropical cyclone naming") and retain their names throughout their lifetimes to provide ease of communication between forecasters and the general public regarding forecasts, watches, and warnings.[[114]](#cite_note-Whats_in_a_name?-114) Since the systems can last a week or longer, and more than one can be occurring in the same basin at the same time, the names are thought to reduce the confusion about what storm is being described.[[114]](#cite_note-Whats_in_a_name?-114) Names are assigned in order from predetermined [lists](/wiki/Lists_of_tropical_cyclone_names "Lists of tropical cyclone names") with one, three, or ten-minute sustained wind speeds of more than 65 km/h (40 mph) depending on which basin it originates.[[19]](#cite_note-RA_IV_HOP-19)[[21]](#cite_note-NIO_TCOP-21)[[22]](#cite_note-SWIO_TCOP-22)

Standards vary from basin to basin. Some tropical depressions are named in the Western Pacific. Tropical cyclones have to have a significant amount of gale-force winds occurring around the center before they are named within the [Southern Hemisphere](/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere "Southern Hemisphere").[[22]](#cite_note-SWIO_TCOP-22)[[23]](#cite_note-SPAC_TCOP-23) The names of significant tropical cyclones in the North Atlantic Ocean, Pacific Ocean, and Australian region are retired from the naming lists and replaced with another name.[[19]](#cite_note-RA_IV_HOP-19)[[20]](#cite_note-WPAC_TCOP-20)[[23]](#cite_note-SPAC_TCOP-23) Tropical cyclones that develop around the world are assigned an identification code consisting of a two-digit number and suffix letter by the warning centers that monitor them.[[23]](#cite_note-SPAC_TCOP-23)[[116]](#cite_note-OFCM_NHOP-116)

### Related cyclone types

See also: [Cyclone](/wiki/Cyclone "Cyclone"), [Extratropical cyclone](/wiki/Extratropical_cyclone "Extratropical cyclone"), and [Subtropical cyclone](/wiki/Subtropical_cyclone "Subtropical cyclone")

In addition to tropical cyclones, there are two other classes of [cyclones](/wiki/Cyclone "Cyclone") within the spectrum of cyclone types. These kinds of cyclones, known as [extratropical cyclones](/wiki/Extratropical_cyclone "Extratropical cyclone") and [subtropical cyclones](/wiki/Subtropical_cyclone "Subtropical cyclone"), can be stages a tropical cyclone passes through during its [formation](/wiki/Tropical_cyclogenesis "Tropical cyclogenesis") or dissipation.[[117]](#cite_note-IWTC5-117) An *extratropical cyclone* is a storm that derives energy from horizontal temperature differences, which are typical in higher latitudes. A tropical cyclone can become extratropical as it moves toward higher latitudes if its energy source changes from heat released by condensation to differences in temperature between air masses. Although not as frequently, an extratropical cyclone can transform into a subtropical storm, and from there into a tropical cyclone.[[118]](#cite_note-autogenerated2-118) From space, extratropical storms have a characteristic "[comma](/wiki/Comma "Comma")-shaped" cloud pattern.[[119]](#cite_note-L14-119) Extratropical cyclones can also be dangerous when their low-pressure centers cause powerful winds and high seas.[[120]](#cite_note-120)

A *subtropical cyclone* is a [weather](/wiki/Weather "Weather") system that has some characteristics of a tropical cyclone and some characteristics of an extratropical cyclone. They can form in a wide band of latitudes, from the equator to 50°. Although subtropical storms rarely have hurricane-force winds, they may become tropical in nature as their cores warm.[[121]](#cite_note-AOML_FAQ_A6-121)

## Structure

### Eye and center

Main article: [Eye (cyclone)](/wiki/Eye_(cyclone) "Eye (cyclone)")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Staring_Down_Hurricane_Florence.jpg/250px-Staring_Down_Hurricane_Florence.jpg)](/wiki/File:Staring_Down_Hurricane_Florence.jpg)

The eye and surrounding clouds of [Hurricane Florence](/wiki/Hurricane_Florence "Hurricane Florence") seen from the [International Space Station](/wiki/International_Space_Station "International Space Station")

At the center of a mature tropical cyclone, air sinks rather than rises. For a sufficiently strong storm, air may sink over a layer deep enough to suppress cloud formation, thereby creating a clear "[eye](/wiki/Eye_(cyclone) "Eye (cyclone)")". Weather in the eye is normally calm and free of [convective clouds](/wiki/Convective_storm "Convective storm"), although the sea may be extremely violent.[[122]](#cite_note-JetStream_structure-122) The eye is normally circular and is typically 30–65 km (19–40 mi) in diameter, though eyes as small as 3 km (1.9 mi) and as large as 370 km (230 mi) have been observed.[[123]](#cite_note-WilmaTCR-123)[[124]](#cite_note-MWR_1996_AHS_summary-124)

The cloudy outer edge of the eye is called the "eyewall". The eyewall typically expands outward with height, resembling an arena football stadium; this phenomenon is sometimes referred to as the "[stadium effect](/wiki/Stadium_effect "Stadium effect")".[[124]](#cite_note-MWR_1996_AHS_summary-124) The eyewall is where the greatest wind speeds are found, air rises most rapidly, clouds reach their highest [altitude](/wiki/Altitude "Altitude"), and precipitation is the heaviest. The heaviest wind damage occurs where a tropical cyclone's eyewall passes over land.[[122]](#cite_note-JetStream_structure-122)

In a weaker storm, the eye may be obscured by the [central dense overcast](/wiki/Central_dense_overcast "Central dense overcast"), which is the upper-level cirrus shield that is associated with a concentrated area of strong thunderstorm activity near the center of a tropical cyclone.[[125]](#cite_note-CDO_AMS-125)

The eyewall may vary over time in the form of [eyewall replacement cycles](/wiki/Eyewall_replacement_cycle "Eyewall replacement cycle"), particularly in intense tropical cyclones. [Outer rainbands](/wiki/Rainbands "Rainbands") can organize into an outer ring of thunderstorms that slowly moves inward, which is believed to rob the primary eyewall of moisture and [angular momentum](/wiki/Angular_momentum "Angular momentum"). When the primary eyewall weakens, the tropical cyclone weakens temporarily. The outer eyewall eventually replaces the primary one at the end of the cycle, at which time the storm may return to its original intensity.[[126]](#cite_note-AOML_FAQ_D8-126)

### Size

Size descriptions of tropical cyclones

| ROCI (Diameter) | Type |
| --- | --- |
| Less than 2 degrees latitude | Very small/minor |
| 2 to 3 degrees of latitude | Small |
| 3 to 6 degrees of latitude | Medium/average/normal |
| 6 to 8 degrees of latitude | Large |
| Over 8 degrees of latitude | Very large[[127]](#cite_note-JTWCsize-127) |

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/1988-_US_Gulf_Coast_hurricane_diameters.svg/250px-1988-_US_Gulf_Coast_hurricane_diameters.svg.png)](/wiki/File:1988-_US_Gulf_Coast_hurricane_diameters.svg)

Though large hurricane size does not imply *strength*—which is based on sustained wind measurements—it can mean that more people are exposed to its hazards.[[128]](#cite_note-WashPost_20240926-128)

There are a variety of metrics commonly used to measure storm size. The most common metrics include the radius of maximum wind, the radius of 34-knot (17 m/s; 63 km/h; 39 mph) wind (i.e. [gale force](/wiki/Gale "Gale")), the radius of outermost closed [isobar](/wiki/Isobar_(meteorology) "Isobar (meteorology)") ([ROCI](/wiki/ROCI "ROCI")), and the radius of vanishing wind.[[129]](#cite_note-Global-129)[[130]](#cite_note-Chavas_Emanuel_GRL-130) An additional metric is the radius at which the cyclone's relative [vorticity](/wiki/Vorticity "Vorticity") field decreases to 1×10−5 s−1.[[124]](#cite_note-MWR_1996_AHS_summary-124)

On Earth, tropical cyclones span a large range of sizes, from 100–2,000 km (62–1,243 mi) as measured by the radius of vanishing wind. They are largest on average in the northwest Pacific Ocean basin and smallest in the northeastern [Pacific Ocean](/wiki/Pacific_Ocean "Pacific Ocean") basin.[[131]](#cite_note-Merrill-131) If the radius of outermost closed isobar is less than two [degrees of latitude](/wiki/Latitude "Latitude") (222 km (138 mi)), then the cyclone is "very small" or a "midget". A radius of 3–6 latitude degrees (333–670 km (207–416 mi)) is considered "average sized". "Very large" tropical cyclones have a radius of greater than 8 degrees (888 km (552 mi)).[[127]](#cite_note-JTWCsize-127) Observations indicate that size is only weakly correlated to variables such as storm intensity (i.e. maximum wind speed), radius of maximum wind, latitude, and maximum potential intensity.[[130]](#cite_note-Chavas_Emanuel_GRL-130)[[131]](#cite_note-Merrill-131) [Typhoon Tip](/wiki/Typhoon_Tip "Typhoon Tip") is the largest cyclone on record, with tropical storm-force winds 2,170 km (1,350 mi) in diameter. The smallest storm on record is [Tropical Storm Marco](/wiki/Tropical_Storm_Marco_(2008) "Tropical Storm Marco (2008)") of [2008](/wiki/2008_Atlantic_hurricane_season "2008 Atlantic hurricane season"), which generated tropical storm-force winds only 37 km (23 mi) in diameter.[[132]](#cite_note-FAQ-132)

## Movement

The movement of a tropical cyclone (i.e. its "track") is typically approximated as the sum of two terms: "steering" by the background environmental wind and "beta drift".[[133]](#cite_note-Holland_JAS_1983-133) Some tropical cyclones can move across large distances, such as [Hurricane John](/wiki/Hurricane_John_(1994) "Hurricane John (1994)"), the second longest-lasting tropical cyclone on record, which traveled 13,280 km (8,250 mi), the longest track of any Northern Hemisphere tropical cyclone, over its 31-day lifespan in [1994](/wiki/1994_Pacific_hurricane_season "1994 Pacific hurricane season").[[134]](#cite_note-john94-134)[[135]](#cite_note-faqe7-135)[[136]](#cite_note-136)

### Environmental steering

Environmental steering is the primary influence on the motion of tropical cyclones.[[137]](#cite_note-Galarneau_and_Davis_2013-137) It represents the movement of the storm due to prevailing winds and other wider environmental conditions, similar to "leaves carried along by a stream".[[138]](#cite_note-AOML_FAQ_G6-138)

Physically, the winds, or [flow field](/w/index.php?title=Flow_field&action=edit&redlink=1 "Flow field (page does not exist)"), in the vicinity of a tropical cyclone may be treated as having two parts: the flow associated with the storm itself, and the large-scale background flow of the environment.[[137]](#cite_note-Galarneau_and_Davis_2013-137) Tropical cyclones can be treated as local maxima of [vorticity](/wiki/Vorticity "Vorticity") suspended within the large-scale background flow of the environment.[[139]](#cite_note-Wu_and_Emmanuel_1995-139) In this way, tropical cyclone motion may be represented to first-order as [advection](/wiki/Advection "Advection") of the storm by the local [environmental flow](/wiki/Environmental_flow "Environmental flow").[[140]](#cite_note-Carr_and_Elsberry_1990-140) This environmental flow is termed the "steering flow" and is the dominant influence on tropical cyclone motion.[[137]](#cite_note-Galarneau_and_Davis_2013-137) The strength and direction of the steering flow can be approximated as a vertical integration of the winds blowing horizontally in the cyclone's vicinity, weighted by the altitude at which those winds are occurring. Because winds can vary with height, determining the steering flow precisely can be difficult.

The [pressure altitude](/wiki/Pressure_altitude "Pressure altitude") at which the background winds are most correlated with a tropical cyclone's motion is known as the "steering level".[[139]](#cite_note-Wu_and_Emmanuel_1995-139) The motion of stronger tropical cyclones is more correlated with the background flow averaged across a thicker portion of [troposphere](/wiki/Troposphere "Troposphere") compared to weaker tropical cyclones whose motion is more correlated with the background flow averaged across a narrower extent of the lower troposphere.[[141]](#cite_note-Velden_and_Leslie_1991-141) When wind shear and [latent heat](/wiki/Latent_heat "Latent heat") release is present, tropical cyclones tend to move towards regions where [potential vorticity](/wiki/Potential_vorticity "Potential vorticity") is increasing most quickly.[[142]](#cite_note-142)

Climatologically, tropical cyclones are steered primarily westward by the east-to-west [trade winds](/wiki/Trade_winds "Trade winds") on the equatorial side of the [subtropical ridge](/wiki/Subtropical_ridge "Subtropical ridge")—a persistent high-pressure area over the world's subtropical oceans.[[138]](#cite_note-AOML_FAQ_G6-138) In the tropical North Atlantic and Northeast Pacific oceans, the trade winds steer [tropical easterly waves](/wiki/Tropical_wave "Tropical wave") westward from the African coast toward the Caribbean Sea, North America, and ultimately into the central Pacific Ocean before the waves dampen out.[[143]](#cite_note-AOML_FAQ_A4-143) These waves are the precursors to many tropical cyclones within this region.[[144]](#cite_note-MWR_Avila_1995-144) In contrast, in the [Indian Ocean](/wiki/Indian_Ocean "Indian Ocean") and Western Pacific in both hemispheres, tropical [cyclogenesis](/wiki/Cyclogenesis "Cyclogenesis") is influenced less by tropical easterly waves and more by the seasonal movement of the Intertropical Convergence Zone and the [monsoon trough](/wiki/Monsoon_trough "Monsoon trough").[[145]](#cite_note-autogenerated1-145) Other weather systems such as mid-latitude [troughs](/wiki/Trough_(meteorology) "Trough (meteorology)") and broad monsoon gyres can also influence tropical cyclone motion by modifying the steering flow.[[141]](#cite_note-Velden_and_Leslie_1991-141)[[146]](#cite_note-Carr_and_Elsberry_1995-146)

### Beta drift

In addition to environmental steering, a tropical cyclone will tend to drift poleward and westward, a motion known as "beta drift".[[147]](#cite_note-Wang_et_al._1998-147) This motion is due to the superposition of a vortex, such as a tropical cyclone, onto an environment in which the [Coriolis force](/wiki/Coriolis_force "Coriolis force") varies with latitude, such as on a sphere or [beta plane](/wiki/Beta_plane "Beta plane").[[148]](#cite_note-Holland_1983-148) The magnitude of the component of tropical cyclone motion associated with the beta drift ranges between 1–3 m/s (3.6–10.8 km/h; 2.2–6.7 mph) and tends to be larger for more intense tropical cyclones and at higher latitudes. It is induced indirectly by the storm itself as a result of feedback between the cyclonic flow of the storm and its environment.[[149]](#cite_note-Fiorino_and_Elsberry_1989-149)[[147]](#cite_note-Wang_et_al._1998-147)

Physically, the cyclonic circulation of the storm advects environmental air poleward east of center and equatorial west of center. Because air must conserve its [angular momentum](/wiki/Absolute_angular_momentum "Absolute angular momentum"), this flow configuration induces a cyclonic gyre equatorward and westward of the storm center and an anticyclonic gyre poleward and eastward of the storm center. The combined flow of these gyres acts to advect the storm slowly poleward and westward. This effect occurs even if there is zero environmental flow.[[150]](#cite_note-Li_and_Wang_1994-150)[[151]](#cite_note-Willoughby_1990-151) Due to a direct dependence of the beta drift on angular momentum, the size of a tropical cyclone can affect the influence of beta drift on its motion; beta drift imparts a greater influence on the movement of larger tropical cyclones than that of smaller ones.[[152]](#cite_note-Hill_and_Lackmann_2009-152)[[153]](#cite_note-Sun_et_al._2015-153)

### Multiple storm interaction

Main article: [Fujiwhara effect](/wiki/Fujiwhara_effect "Fujiwhara effect")

A third component of motion that occurs relatively infrequently involves the interaction of multiple tropical cyclones. When two cyclones approach one another, their centers will begin orbiting cyclonically about a point between the two systems. Depending on their separation distance and strength, the two vortices may simply orbit around one another, or else may spiral into the center point and merge. When the two vortices are of unequal size, the larger vortex will tend to dominate the interaction, and the smaller vortex will orbit around it. This phenomenon is called the Fujiwhara effect, after [Sakuhei Fujiwhara](/wiki/Sakuhei_Fujiwhara "Sakuhei Fujiwhara").[[154]](#cite_note-154)

### Interaction with the mid-latitude westerlies

See also: [Westerlies](/wiki/Westerlies "Westerlies")

[![Path of a tropical cyclone](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Ioke_2006_track.png/250px-Ioke_2006_track.png)](/wiki/File:Ioke_2006_track.png)

Storm track of [Typhoon Ioke](/wiki/Hurricane_Ioke "Hurricane Ioke"), showing [recurvature](/wiki/Recurvature "Recurvature") off the [Japanese coast](/wiki/Japanese_coastline "Japanese coastline") in [2006](/wiki/2006_Pacific_hurricane_season "2006 Pacific hurricane season")

Though a tropical cyclone typically moves from east to west in the tropics, its track may shift poleward and eastward either as it moves west of the subtropical ridge axis or else if it interacts with the mid-latitude flow, such as the [jet stream](/wiki/Jet_stream "Jet stream") or an [extratropical cyclone](/wiki/Extratropical_cyclone "Extratropical cyclone"). This motion, termed "[recurvature](/wiki/Recurvature "Recurvature")", commonly occurs near the western edge of the major ocean basins, where the jet stream typically has a poleward component and extratropical cyclones are common.[[155]](#cite_note-155) An example of tropical cyclone recurvature was [Typhoon Ioke](/wiki/Typhoon_Ioke "Typhoon Ioke") in 2006.[[156]](#cite_note-CPHC_Ioke-156)

## Effects

Main articles: [Effects of tropical cyclones](/wiki/Effects_of_tropical_cyclones "Effects of tropical cyclones") and [Tropical cyclone effects by region](/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_effects_by_region "Tropical cyclone effects by region")

### Natural phenomena caused or worsened by tropical cyclones

Tropical cyclones out at sea cause large waves, [heavy rain](/wiki/Heavy_rain_(meteorology) "Heavy rain (meteorology)"), [floods](/wiki/Flood "Flood") and high winds, disrupting international shipping and, at times, causing shipwrecks.[[157]](#cite_note-18cva-157) Tropical cyclones stir up water, leaving a cool wake behind them, which causes the region to be less favorable for subsequent tropical cyclones.[[67]](#cite_note-NASA_Cooling-67) On land, strong [winds](/wiki/Wind "Wind") can damage or destroy vehicles, buildings, bridges, and other outside objects, turning loose debris into deadly flying projectiles. The [storm surge](/wiki/Storm_surge "Storm surge"), or the increase in sea level due to the cyclone, is typically the worst effect from landfalling tropical cyclones, historically resulting in 90% of tropical cyclone deaths.[[158]](#cite_note-Shultz_Epid_Reviews_2005-158) [Cyclone Mahina](/wiki/Cyclone_Mahina "Cyclone Mahina") produced the highest storm surge on record, 13 m (43 ft), at [Bathurst Bay](/wiki/Bathurst_Bay "Bathurst Bay"), [Queensland](/wiki/Queensland "Queensland"), [Australia](/wiki/Australia "Australia"), in March 1899.[[159]](#cite_note-159)

Other ocean-based hazards that tropical cyclones produce are [rip currents](/wiki/Rip_current "Rip current") and [undertow](/wiki/Undertow_(water_waves) "Undertow (water waves)"). These hazards can occur hundreds of kilometers (hundreds of miles) away from the center of a cyclone, even if other weather conditions are favorable.[[160]](#cite_note-160)[[161]](#cite_note-161)
The broad rotation of a landfalling tropical cyclone, and vertical wind shear at its periphery, spawns [tornadoes](/wiki/List_of_tropical_cyclone-spawned_tornadoes "List of tropical cyclone-spawned tornadoes"). Tornadoes can also be spawned as a result of [eyewall mesovortices](/wiki/Eyewall_mesovortices "Eyewall mesovortices"), which persist until landfall.[[162]](#cite_note-AOML_FAQ_L6-162) [Hurricane Ivan](/wiki/Hurricane_Ivan "Hurricane Ivan") produced [120 tornadoes](/wiki/Hurricane_Ivan_tornado_outbreak "Hurricane Ivan tornado outbreak"), more than any other tropical cyclone.[[163]](#cite_note-163) Lightning activity is produced within tropical cyclones. This activity is more intense within stronger storms and closer to and within the storm's eyewall.[[164]](#cite_note-164)[[165]](#cite_note-165) Tropical cyclones can increase the amount of snowfall a region experiences by delivering additional moisture.[[166]](#cite_note-166) Wildfires can be worsened when a nearby storm fans their flames with its strong winds.[[167]](#cite_note-167)[[168]](#cite_note-168)

### Effect on property and human life

[![total collapse of houses, cars and facilities](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Hurricane_Ike_Bolivar_Peninsula%2C_TX.jpg/250px-Hurricane_Ike_Bolivar_Peninsula%2C_TX.jpg)](/wiki/File:Hurricane_Ike_Bolivar_Peninsula,_TX.jpg)

Aftermath of [Hurricane Ike](/wiki/Hurricane_Ike "Hurricane Ike") in [Bolivar Peninsula, Texas](/wiki/Bolivar_Peninsula,_Texas "Bolivar Peninsula, Texas")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/1980-_Cost_of_billion_dollar_hurricanes_-_US_-_variwide_chart_-_NOAA_data.svg/250px-1980-_Cost_of_billion_dollar_hurricanes_-_US_-_variwide_chart_-_NOAA_data.svg.png)](/wiki/File:1980-_Cost_of_billion_dollar_hurricanes_-_US_-_variwide_chart_-_NOAA_data.svg)

The number of $1 billion Atlantic hurricanes almost doubled from the 1980s to the 2010s, and inflation-adjusted costs have increased more than elevenfold.[[169]](#cite_note-NYTimes_20221202-169) The increases have been attributed to climate change and to greater numbers of people moving to coastal areas.[[169]](#cite_note-NYTimes_20221202-169)

Tropical cyclones regularly affect the coastlines of most of [Earth](/wiki/Earth "Earth")'s major bodies of water along the [Atlantic](/wiki/Atlantic "Atlantic"), [Pacific](/wiki/Pacific "Pacific"), and [Indian](/wiki/Indian_Ocean "Indian Ocean") oceans. Tropical cyclones have caused significant destruction and loss of human life, resulting in about 2 million deaths since the 19th century.[[170]](#cite_note-red-170) Large areas of standing water caused by flooding lead to [infection](/wiki/Infection "Infection"), as well as contributing to [mosquito-borne illnesses](/wiki/Mosquito-borne_disease "Mosquito-borne disease"). Crowded evacuees in [shelters](/wiki/Emergency_shelter "Emergency shelter") increase the risk of disease propagation.[[158]](#cite_note-Shultz_Epid_Reviews_2005-158) Tropical cyclones significantly interrupt infrastructure, leading to [power outages](/wiki/Power_outage "Power outage"), bridge and road destruction, and the hampering of reconstruction efforts.[[158]](#cite_note-Shultz_Epid_Reviews_2005-158)[[171]](#cite_note-Power_failures-171)[[172]](#cite_note-172)

Winds and water from storms can damage or destroy homes, buildings, and other manmade structures.[[173]](#cite_note-173)[[174]](#cite_note-174) Tropical cyclones destroy agriculture, kill livestock, and prevent access to marketplaces for both buyers and sellers; both of these result in financial losses.[[175]](#cite_note-175)[[176]](#cite_note-176)[[177]](#cite_note-177) Powerful cyclones that make [landfall](/wiki/Landfall "Landfall") – moving from the ocean to over land – are some of the most powerful, although that is not always the case. An average of 86 tropical cyclones of tropical storm intensity form annually worldwide, with 47 reaching hurricane or typhoon strength, and 20 becoming intense tropical cyclones, super typhoons, or major hurricanes (at least of [Category 3](/wiki/Saffir-Simpson_Hurricane_Scale "Saffir-Simpson Hurricane Scale") intensity).[[178]](#cite_note-Landsea_variability_table-178)

#### Africa

In [Africa](/wiki/Africa "Africa"), tropical cyclones can originate from [tropical waves](/wiki/Tropical_wave "Tropical wave") generated over the [Sahara Desert](/wiki/Sahara_Desert "Sahara Desert"),[[179]](#cite_note-179) or otherwise strike the [Horn of Africa](/wiki/Horn_of_Africa "Horn of Africa") and [Southern Africa](/wiki/Southern_Africa "Southern Africa").[[180]](#cite_note-180)[[181]](#cite_note-181) [Cyclone Idai](/wiki/Cyclone_Idai "Cyclone Idai") in March 2019 hit central [Mozambique](/wiki/Mozambique "Mozambique"), becoming the deadliest tropical cyclone on record in Africa, with 1,302 fatalities, and damage estimated at US$2.2 billion.[[182]](#cite_note-182)[[183]](#cite_note-Global_Catastrophe_Recap-183) [Réunion](/wiki/R%C3%A9union "Réunion") island, located east of Southern Africa, experiences some of the wettest tropical cyclones on record. In January 1980, [Cyclone Hyacinthe](/wiki/Cyclone_Hyacinthe "Cyclone Hyacinthe") produced 6,083 mm (239.5 in) of rain over 15 days, which was the largest rain total recorded from a tropical cyclone on record.[[184]](#cite_note-184)[[185]](#cite_note-185)[[186]](#cite_note-186)

#### Asia

In [Asia](/wiki/Asia "Asia"), tropical cyclones from the Indian and Pacific oceans regularly affect some of the most populated countries on Earth. In 1970, [a cyclone](/wiki/1970_Bhola_cyclone "1970 Bhola cyclone") struck [Bangladesh](/wiki/Bangladesh "Bangladesh"), then known as East Pakistan, producing a 6.1 m (20 ft) storm surge that killed at least 300,000 people. This made it the deadliest tropical cyclone on record.[[187]](#cite_note-dead-187) In October 2019, [Typhoon Hagibis](/wiki/Typhoon_Hagibis "Typhoon Hagibis") struck the [Japanese](/wiki/Japan "Japan") island of [Honshu](/wiki/Honshu "Honshu") and inflicted US$15 billion in damage, making it the costliest storm on record in Japan.[[188]](#cite_note-188) The islands that comprise [Oceania](/wiki/Oceania "Oceania"), from [Australia](/wiki/Australia "Australia") to [French Polynesia](/wiki/French_Polynesia "French Polynesia"), are routinely affected by tropical cyclones.[[189]](#cite_note-189)[[190]](#cite_note-190)[[191]](#cite_note-191) In [Indonesia](/wiki/Indonesia "Indonesia"), [a cyclone](/wiki/1973_Flores_cyclone "1973 Flores cyclone") struck the island of [Flores](/wiki/Flores "Flores") in April 1973, killing 1,653 people, making it the deadliest tropical cyclone recorded in the [Southern Hemisphere](/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere "Southern Hemisphere").[[192]](#cite_note-192)[[193]](#cite_note-193)

#### North and South America

[Atlantic](/wiki/Atlantic_hurricane "Atlantic hurricane") and [Pacific hurricanes](/wiki/Pacific_hurricanes "Pacific hurricanes") regularly affect [North America](/wiki/North_America "North America"). In the [United States](/wiki/United_States "United States"), hurricanes [Katrina](/wiki/Hurricane_Katrina "Hurricane Katrina") in 2005 and [Harvey](/wiki/Hurricane_Harvey "Hurricane Harvey") in 2017 are the country's costliest ever natural disasters, with monetary damage estimated at US$125 billion. Katrina struck [Louisiana](/wiki/Louisiana "Louisiana") and largely destroyed the city of [New Orleans](/wiki/New_Orleans "New Orleans"),[[194]](#cite_note-194)[[195]](#cite_note-Harvey_TCR-195) while Harvey caused significant flooding in southeastern [Texas](/wiki/Texas "Texas") after it dropped 60.58 in (1,539 mm) of rainfall; this was the highest rainfall total on record in the country.[[195]](#cite_note-Harvey_TCR-195)

The Caribbean islands are regularly hit by hurricanes, which have caused multiple humanitarian crises in [Haiti](/wiki/Haiti "Haiti") since 2004 due in part to the lack of infrastructure and high population density in urban areas.[[196]](#cite_note-196)[[197]](#cite_note-197) In 2004, hurricane [Jeanne](/wiki/Hurricane_Jeanne "Hurricane Jeanne") caused severe flooding and mudslides, and a total estimated 3,006 deaths.[[198]](#cite_note-198) More recently, in 2016, hurricane [Matthew](/wiki/Hurricane_Matthew "Hurricane Matthew") caused US$2.8 billion in damages, killing an estimated 674 people.[[199]](#cite_note-199)[[200]](#cite_note-200)

The northern portion of [South America](/wiki/South_America "South America") experiences occasional tropical cyclones, with 173 fatalities from [Tropical Storm Bret](/wiki/Tropical_Storm_Bret_(1993) "Tropical Storm Bret (1993)") in August 1993.[[201]](#cite_note-201)[[202]](#cite_note-202) The [South Atlantic Ocean](/wiki/South_Atlantic_Ocean "South Atlantic Ocean") is generally inhospitable to the formation of a tropical storm.[[203]](#cite_note-203) However, in March 2004, [Hurricane Catarina](/wiki/Hurricane_Catarina "Hurricane Catarina") struck southeastern [Brazil](/wiki/Brazil "Brazil") as the first hurricane on record in the South Atlantic Ocean.[[204]](#cite_note-204)

#### Europe

[Europe](/wiki/Europe "Europe") is rarely affected by tropical cyclones; however, the continent regularly encounters storms after they transitioned into [extratropical cyclones](/wiki/Extratropical_cyclone "Extratropical cyclone"). Only one tropical depression – [Vince](/wiki/Hurricane_Vince "Hurricane Vince") in 2005 – struck [Spain](/wiki/Spain "Spain"),[[205]](#cite_note-205) and only one [subtropical cyclone](/wiki/Subtropical_cyclone "Subtropical cyclone") – [Subtropical Storm Alpha](/wiki/Subtropical_Storm_Alpha_(2020) "Subtropical Storm Alpha (2020)") in 2020 – struck [Portugal](/wiki/Portugal "Portugal").[[206]](#cite_note-206) Occasionally, there are [tropical-like cyclones](/wiki/Mediterranean_tropical-like_cyclone "Mediterranean tropical-like cyclone") in the [Mediterranean Sea](/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea "Mediterranean Sea").[[207]](#cite_note-207)

### Environmental effects

Although cyclones take an enormous toll in lives and personal property, they may be important factors in the [precipitation](/wiki/Precipitation "Precipitation") regimes of places they affect, as they may bring much-needed precipitation to otherwise dry regions.[[208]](#cite_note-2005_EPac_outlook-208) Their precipitation may also alleviate drought conditions by restoring soil moisture, though one study focused on the [Southeastern United States](/wiki/Southeastern_United_States "Southeastern United States") suggested tropical cyclones did not offer significant drought recovery.[[209]](#cite_note-DontFix-209)[[210]](#cite_note-Yoo_et_al._(2015)-210)[[211]](#cite_note-Kam_et_al._(2013)-211) Tropical cyclones also help maintain the global heat balance by moving warm, moist tropical air to the [middle latitudes](/wiki/Middle_latitudes "Middle latitudes") and [polar regions](/wiki/Polar_regions "Polar regions"),[[212]](#cite_note-JetStream_introduction-212) and by regulating the [thermohaline circulation](/wiki/Thermohaline_circulation "Thermohaline circulation") through [upwelling](/wiki/Upwelling "Upwelling").[[213]](#cite_note-213) Research on Pacific cyclones has demonstrated that deeper layers of the ocean receive a [heat transfer](/wiki/Heat_transfer_physics "Heat transfer physics") from these powerful storms.[[214]](#cite_note-214)[[215]](#cite_note-215)

The storm surge and winds of hurricanes may be destructive to human-made structures, but they also stir up the waters of coastal [estuaries](/wiki/Estuaries "Estuaries"), which are typically important [fish breeding](/wiki/Fish_reproduction "Fish reproduction") locales.[[216]](#cite_note-Christopherson-216) Ecosystems, such as [saltmarshes](/wiki/Saltmarsh "Saltmarsh") and [Mangrove forests](/wiki/Mangrove_forest "Mangrove forest"), can be severely damaged or destroyed by tropical cyclones, which erode land and destroy vegetation.[[217]](#cite_note-217)[[218]](#cite_note-218) Tropical cyclones can cause harmful [algae blooms](/wiki/Algae_bloom "Algae bloom") to form in bodies of water by increasing the amount of nutrients available.[[219]](#cite_note-Oil+Algae-219)[[220]](#cite_note-220)[[221]](#cite_note-221) Insect populations can decrease in both quantity and diversity after the passage of storms.[[222]](#cite_note-222) Strong winds associated with tropical cyclones and their remnants are capable of felling thousands of trees, causing damage to forests.[[223]](#cite_note-223)

When hurricanes surge upon shore from the ocean, salt is introduced to many freshwater areas and raises the [salinity](/wiki/Salinity "Salinity") levels too high for some habitats to withstand. Some are able to cope with the salt and recycle it back into the ocean, but others can not release the extra surface water quickly enough or do not have a large enough freshwater source to replace it. Because of this, some species of plants and vegetation die due to the excess salt.[[224]](#cite_note-224) Hurricanes can carry [toxins](/wiki/Toxins "Toxins") and [acids](/wiki/Acids "Acids") onshore when they make landfall. The floodwater can pick up the toxins from different spills and contaminate the land that it passes over. These toxins are harmful to the people and animals in the area, as well as the environment around them.[[225]](#cite_note-225) Tropical cyclones can cause [oil spills](/wiki/Oil_spill "Oil spill") by damaging or destroying pipelines and storage facilities.[[226]](#cite_note-226)[[219]](#cite_note-Oil+Algae-219)[[227]](#cite_note-Oil_+_Chemicals-227) Similarly, chemical spills have been reported when chemical and processing facilities were damaged.[[227]](#cite_note-Oil_+_Chemicals-227)[[228]](#cite_note-228)[[229]](#cite_note-229) Waterways have become contaminated with toxic levels of metals such as [nickel](/wiki/Nickel "Nickel"), [chromium](/wiki/Chromium "Chromium"), and [mercury](/wiki/Mercury_(element) "Mercury (element)") during tropical cyclones.[[230]](#cite_note-230)[[231]](#cite_note-Erosion_+_Mercury-231)

Tropical cyclones can have an extensive effect on geography, such as creating or destroying land.[[232]](#cite_note-Land_created-232)[[233]](#cite_note-Land_Destroyed-233) [Cyclone Bebe](/wiki/Cyclone_Bebe "Cyclone Bebe") increased the size of [Tuvalu](/wiki/Tuvalu "Tuvalu") island, [Funafuti Atoll](/wiki/Funafuti_Atoll "Funafuti Atoll"), by nearly 20%.[[232]](#cite_note-Land_created-232)[[234]](#cite_note-234)[[235]](#cite_note-235) [Hurricane Walaka](/wiki/Hurricane_Walaka "Hurricane Walaka") destroyed the small [East Island](/wiki/East_Island_(Hawaii) "East Island (Hawaii)") in 2018,[[233]](#cite_note-Land_Destroyed-233)[[236]](#cite_note-236) which destroyed the habitat for the endangered [Hawaiian monk seal](/wiki/Hawaiian_monk_seal "Hawaiian monk seal"), as well as, threatened [sea turtles](/wiki/Sea_turtle "Sea turtle") and [seabirds](/wiki/Seabird "Seabird").[[237]](#cite_note-237) [Landslides](/wiki/Landslide "Landslide") frequently occur during tropical cyclones and can vastly alter landscapes. Some storms are capable of causing hundreds to tens of thousands of landslides.[[238]](#cite_note-238)[[239]](#cite_note-239)[[240]](#cite_note-240)[[241]](#cite_note-241) Storms can erode coastlines over an extensive area and transport the sediment to other locations.[[231]](#cite_note-Erosion_+_Mercury-231)[[242]](#cite_note-242)[[243]](#cite_note-243)

## Observation and forecasting

### Observation

Main article: [Tropical cyclone observation](/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_observation "Tropical cyclone observation")

[![Aerial view of storm clouds](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Isidore091902-p3sunset.jpg/330px-Isidore091902-p3sunset.jpg)](/wiki/File:Isidore091902-p3sunset.jpg)

A sunset view of [Hurricane Isidore](/wiki/Hurricane_Isidore "Hurricane Isidore")'s [rainbands](/wiki/Rainband "Rainband") photographed at 2,100 m (7,000 ft)

[![Head-on view of an airplane](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/NOAA_Lockheed_WP-3D_Orion_%22Hurricane_Hunter%22_%28N42RF%29_at_SENEX_Air_Quality-Climate_Research_Study_%289119691512%29.jpg/250px-NOAA_Lockheed_WP-3D_Orion_%22Hurricane_Hunter%22_%28N42RF%29_at_SENEX_Air_Quality-Climate_Research_Study_%289119691512%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:NOAA_Lockheed_WP-3D_Orion_%22Hurricane_Hunter%22_(N42RF)_at_SENEX_Air_Quality-Climate_Research_Study_(9119691512).jpg)

"[Hurricane Hunter](/wiki/Hurricane_hunters "Hurricane hunters")" – [WP-3D Orion](/wiki/WP-3D_Orion "WP-3D Orion") is used to go into the [eye of a hurricane](/wiki/Eye_(cyclone) "Eye (cyclone)") for data collection and measurements purposes.

Tropical cyclones have occurred around the world for millennia. Reanalyses and research are being undertaken to extend the historical record, through the usage of [proxy data](/wiki/Proxy_(climate) "Proxy (climate)") such as overwash deposits, [beach ridges](/wiki/Beach_ridge "Beach ridge") and historical documents such as diaries.[[244]](#cite_note-6000_WA-244) Major tropical cyclones leave traces in [overwash records](/wiki/Overwash "Overwash") and shell layers in some coastal areas, which have been used to gain insight into hurricane activity over the past thousands of years.[[245]](#cite_note-:8-245) Sediment records in Western Australia suggest an intense tropical cyclone in the [4th millennium BC](/wiki/4th_millennium_BC "4th millennium BC").[[244]](#cite_note-6000_WA-244)

Proxy records based on [paleotempestological](/wiki/Paleotempestology "Paleotempestology") research have revealed that major hurricane activity along the [Gulf of Mexico](/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico "Gulf of Mexico") coast varies on timescales of centuries to millennia.[[246]](#cite_note-Liu1999-246)[[247]](#cite_note-LiuFearn2000-247) In the year 957, a powerful typhoon struck [southern China](/wiki/South_China "South China"), killing around 10,000 people due to flooding.[[248]](#cite_note-248) The [Spanish colonization of Mexico](/wiki/New_Spain "New Spain") described "tempestades" in 1730,[[249]](#cite_note-calif-249) although the official record for Pacific hurricanes only dates to 1949.[[250]](#cite_note-HURDAT-250) In the south-west Indian Ocean, the tropical cyclone record goes back to 1848.[[251]](#cite_note-251) In 2003, the [Atlantic hurricane reanalysis project](/wiki/Atlantic_hurricane_reanalysis_project "Atlantic hurricane reanalysis project") examined and analyzed the historical record of tropical cyclones in the Atlantic back to 1851, extending the existing database from 1886.[[252]](#cite_note-252)

Before satellite imagery became available during the 20th century, many of these systems went undetected unless it impacted land or a ship encountered it by chance.[[1]](#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-1) Often in part because of the threat of hurricanes, many coastal regions had sparse population between major ports until the advent of automobile tourism; therefore, the most severe portions of hurricanes striking the coast may have gone unmeasured in some instances. The combined effects of ship destruction and remote landfall severely limit the number of intense hurricanes in the official record before the era of hurricane reconnaissance aircraft and satellite meteorology. Although the record shows a distinct increase in the number and strength of intense hurricanes, therefore, experts regard the early data as suspect.[[253]](#cite_note-BOM_TC_Guide_1.3-253) The ability of climatologists to make a long-term analysis of tropical cyclones is limited by the amount of reliable historical data.[[254]](#cite_note-254)

In the 1940s, routine aircraft reconnaissance started in both the Atlantic and Western Pacific basin in the mid-1940s, which provided ground truth data. Early flights were only made once or twice a day.[[1]](#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-1) In 1960, Polar-orbiting weather satellites were first launched by the United States [National Aeronautics and Space Administration](/wiki/National_Aeronautics_and_Space_Administration "National Aeronautics and Space Administration"), but were not declared operational until 1965.[[1]](#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-1) It took several years for some of the warning centers to take advantage of this new viewing platform and develop the expertise to associate satellite signatures with storm position and intensity.[[1]](#cite_note-Global_Guide_2017-1)

Intense tropical cyclones pose a particular observation challenge, as they are a dangerous oceanic phenomenon, and [weather stations](/wiki/Weather_station "Weather station"), being relatively sparse, are rarely available on the site of the storm itself. In general, surface observations are available only if the storm is passing over an island or a coastal area, or if there is a nearby ship. Real-time measurements are usually taken in the periphery of the cyclone, where conditions are less catastrophic and its true strength cannot be evaluated. For this reason, there are teams of meteorologists that move into the path of tropical cyclones to help evaluate their strength at the point of landfall.[[255]](#cite_note-FCMP-255)

Tropical cyclones are tracked by [weather satellites](/wiki/Weather_satellite "Weather satellite") capturing [visible](/wiki/Visible_light "Visible light") and [infrared](/wiki/Infrared "Infrared") images from space, usually at half-hour to quarter-hour intervals. As a storm approaches land, it can be observed by land-based [Doppler weather radar](/wiki/Doppler_weather_radar "Doppler weather radar"). Radar plays a crucial role around landfall by showing a storm's location and intensity every several minutes.[[256]](#cite_note-CPHC-256) Other satellites provide information from the perturbations of [GPS](/wiki/GPS "GPS") signals, providing thousands of snapshots per day and capturing atmospheric temperature, pressure, and moisture content.[[257]](#cite_note-257)

[In situ](/wiki/In_situ "In situ") measurements, in real-time, can be taken by sending specially equipped reconnaissance flights into the cyclone. In the Atlantic basin, these flights are regularly flown by United States government [hurricane hunters](/wiki/Hurricane_hunters "Hurricane hunters").[[258]](#cite_note-Hurricane_Hunters-258) These aircraft fly directly into the cyclone and take direct and remote-sensing measurements. The aircraft launch [GPS dropsondes](/wiki/GPS_dropsonde "GPS dropsonde") inside the cyclone. These sondes measure temperature, humidity, pressure, and especially winds between flight level and the ocean's surface. A new era in hurricane observation began when a remotely piloted [Aerosonde](/wiki/Insitu_Aerosonde "Insitu Aerosonde"), a small drone aircraft, was flown through [Tropical Storm Ophelia](/wiki/Tropical_Storm_Ophelia_(2005) "Tropical Storm Ophelia (2005)") as it passed [Virginia](/wiki/Virginia "Virginia")'s eastern shore during the [2005 hurricane season](/wiki/2005_hurricane_season "2005 hurricane season"). A similar mission was also completed successfully in the western Pacific Ocean.[[259]](#cite_note-SunHerald-259)

### Forecasting

See also: [Tropical cyclone track forecasting](/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_track_forecasting "Tropical cyclone track forecasting"), [Tropical cyclone prediction model](/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_prediction_model "Tropical cyclone prediction model"), and [Tropical cyclone rainfall forecasting](/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_rainfall_forecasting "Tropical cyclone rainfall forecasting")

[![A graph shows five colored curves (actually, jagged point-to-point data sets) measuring average forecast errors in nautical miles (0 to 700, the y-axis on the left) for each year (from 1970 to 2022, the x-axis at the bottom). The red curve indicates forecast errors 24 hours in advance, and is the lowest of the five curves; its points and the resultant trend line are below that of the other curves. The 24-hour forecast trends from approximately 140 nm in 1970 to about 45 nm in 2022. The green line shows forecast errors 48 hours in advance, with a trend line from about 290 nm in 1970 to 45 nm in 2022. The yellow curve indicates errors from 72-hour forecasts, and jags dramatically up and down in the first 10 years shown. Its trend line runs from approx. 445 nm (1970) to about 50 nm (2022). The two remaining lines stretch only from 2001. The brown curve shows a 96-hour forecast (trending from about 240 nm in 2001 to 100 nm in 2022), and the blue line for forecasts 120 hours in advance trends from about 310 nm (2001) to 150 nm (2022). With remarkable consistency, the farther in advance the forecast is, the greater the error visible here, and the trend line for each set of plotted points is clearly downward, generally with increasing steepness for the wider-ranging forecasts.](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/NHC_Atlantic_Forecast_Error_Trends.png/330px-NHC_Atlantic_Forecast_Error_Trends.png)](/wiki/File:NHC_Atlantic_Forecast_Error_Trends.png)

A general decrease in error trends in tropical cyclone path prediction is evident since the 1970s.

High-speed computers and sophisticated simulation software allow forecasters to produce [computer models](/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_prediction_model "Tropical cyclone prediction model") that predict tropical cyclone tracks based on the future position and strength of high- and low-pressure systems. Combining forecast models with increased understanding of the forces that act on tropical cyclones, as well as with a wealth of data from Earth-orbiting [satellites](/wiki/Satellites "Satellites") and other sensors, scientists have increased the accuracy of track forecasts over recent decades.[[260]](#cite_note-NHC_forecast_verifications_models-260)

However, scientists are not as skillful at predicting the intensity of tropical cyclones.[[261]](#cite_note-NHC_forecast_verifications_Atlantic-261) The lack of improvement in intensity forecasting is attributed to the complexity of tropical systems and an incomplete understanding of factors that affect their development. New tropical cyclone position and forecast information is available at least every six hours from the various warning centers.[[262]](#cite_note-WMO_RSMC_list-262)[[263]](#cite_note-263)[[264]](#cite_note-264)[[265]](#cite_note-NHCprod-265)[[266]](#cite_note-266)

### Geopotential height

Main article: [Geopotential height](/wiki/Geopotential_height "Geopotential height")

In meteorology, geopotential heights are used when creating forecasts and analyzing pressure systems. Geopotential heights represent the estimate of the real height of a pressure system above the average sea level.[[267]](#cite_note-Geo_Height-267) Geopotential heights for weather are divided up into several levels. The lowest geopotential height level is 850 hPa (25.10 inHg), which represents the lowest 1,500 m (5,000 ft) of the atmosphere. The moisture content, gained by using either the relative humidity or the precipitable water value, is used in creating forecasts for precipitation.[[268]](#cite_note-268)

The next level, 700 hPa (20.67 inHg), is at a height of 2,300–3,200 m (7,700–10,500 ft). 700 hPa is regarded as the highest point in the lower atmosphere. At this layer, both vertical movement and moisture levels are used to locate and create forecasts for precipitation.[[269]](#cite_note-269) The middle level of the atmosphere is at 500 hPa (14.76 inHg) or a height of 4,900–6,100 m (16,000–20,000 ft). The 500 hPa level is used for measuring atmospheric vorticity, commonly known as the spin of air. The relative humidity is also analyzed at this height to establish where precipitation is likely to materialize.[[270]](#cite_note-270) The next level occurs at 300 hPa (8.859 inHg) or a height of 8,200–9,800 m (27,000–32,000 ft).[[271]](#cite_note-271) The top-most level is located at 200 hPa (5.906 inHg), which corresponds to a height of 11,000–12,000 m (35,000–41,000 ft). Both the 200 and 300 hPa levels are mainly used to locate the jet stream.[[272]](#cite_note-272)

## Awareness and response

### Preparations

Main articles: [Tropical cyclone preparedness](/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_preparedness "Tropical cyclone preparedness") and [Tropical cyclone engineering](/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_engineering "Tropical cyclone engineering")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Hurricane_Route_sign_Tulane_Avenue_floodlines.jpg/250px-Hurricane_Route_sign_Tulane_Avenue_floodlines.jpg)](/wiki/File:Hurricane_Route_sign_Tulane_Avenue_floodlines.jpg)

Evacuation route sign on Tulane Avenue in [New Orleans](/wiki/New_Orleans "New Orleans") shows lines from long standing floodwaters after [Hurricane Katrina](/wiki/Hurricane_Katrina "Hurricane Katrina").

Ahead of the formal season starting, people are urged to [prepare for the effects of a tropical cyclone](/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_preparedness "Tropical cyclone preparedness") by politicians and weather forecasters, among others. They prepare by determining their risk to the different types of weather, tropical cyclones cause, checking their insurance coverage and emergency supplies, as well as determining where to evacuate to if needed.[[273]](#cite_note-ReadyGov_Preparedness-273)[[274]](#cite_note-Gray_et_al._2019-274)[[275]](#cite_note-Morrissey_and_Reser_2003-275) When a tropical cyclone develops and is forecast to impact land, each member nation of the [World Meteorological Organization](/wiki/World_Meteorological_Organization "World Meteorological Organization") issues various [watches and warnings](/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_watches_and_warnings "Tropical cyclone watches and warnings") to cover the expected effects.[[276]](#cite_note-WMO_TC-276) However, there are some exceptions with the United States National Hurricane Center and Fiji Meteorological Service responsible for issuing or recommending warnings for other nations in their area of responsibility.[[277]](#cite_note-AboutFMS-277)[[278]](#cite_note-About_NHC-278)[[279]](#cite_note-RAIV2017-279): 2–4

An important decision in individual preparedness is determining if and when to evacuate an area that will be affected by a tropical cyclone.[[280]](#cite_note-280) [Tropical cyclone tracking charts](/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_tracking_chart "Tropical cyclone tracking chart") allow people to track ongoing systems to form their own opinions regarding where the storms are going and whether or not they need to prepare for the system being tracked, including possible evacuation. This continues to be encouraged by the [National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration](/wiki/National_Oceanic_and_Atmospheric_Administration "National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration") and National Hurricane Center.[[281]](#cite_note-FTH-281)

### Response

Main article: [Tropical cyclone response](/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_response "Tropical cyclone response")

|  |  |
| --- | --- |
| [[icon]](/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg) | This section **needs expansion**. You can help by [making an edit request](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Talk%3ATropical_cyclone&preload=Template%3ASubmit+an+edit+request%2Fpreload&action=edit&section=new&editintro=Template%3AEdit+protected%2Feditintro&preloadtitle=Protected+edit+request+on+7+November+2025&preloadparams%5B%5D=edit+fully-protected&preloadparams%5B%5D=Tropical+cyclone)[adding to it](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Tropical_cyclone&action=edit&section=) . *(October 2022)* |

[![View of tropical cyclone damage from a helicopter](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/DSS_Supports_Hurricane_Dorian_Relief_Efforts_in_the_Bahamas_%2849091289561%29.jpg/250px-DSS_Supports_Hurricane_Dorian_Relief_Efforts_in_the_Bahamas_%2849091289561%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:DSS_Supports_Hurricane_Dorian_Relief_Efforts_in_the_Bahamas_(49091289561).jpg)

Relief efforts for [Hurricane Dorian](/wiki/Hurricane_Dorian "Hurricane Dorian") in [the Bahamas](/wiki/The_Bahamas "The Bahamas")

Hurricane response is the [disaster response](/wiki/Disaster_response "Disaster response") after a hurricane. Activities performed by hurricane responders include assessment, restoration, and demolition of buildings; removal of [debris](/wiki/Debris "Debris") and waste; repairs to land-based and maritime [infrastructure](/wiki/Infrastructure "Infrastructure"); and public health services including [search and rescue](/wiki/Search_and_rescue "Search and rescue") operations.[[282]](#cite_note-282) Hurricane response requires coordination between federal, tribal, state, local, and private entities.[[283]](#cite_note-283) According to the [National Voluntary Organizations Active in Disaster](/wiki/National_Voluntary_Organizations_Active_in_Disaster "National Voluntary Organizations Active in Disaster"), potential response volunteers should affiliate with established organizations and should not self-deploy, so that proper training and support can be provided to mitigate the danger and stress of response work.[[284]](#cite_note-284)

Hurricane responders face many hazards. Hurricane responders may be exposed to chemical and biological contaminants including stored chemicals, [sewage](/wiki/Sewage "Sewage"), [human remains](/wiki/Corpse "Corpse"), and [mold](/wiki/Indoor_mold "Indoor mold") growth encouraged by flooding,[[285]](#cite_note-:0-285)[[286]](#cite_note-:2-286)[[287]](#cite_note-287) as well as [asbestos](/wiki/Asbestos "Asbestos") and [lead](/wiki/Lead "Lead") that may be present in older buildings.[[286]](#cite_note-:2-286)[[288]](#cite_note-:1-288) Common injuries arise from [falls](/wiki/Falling_(accident) "Falling (accident)") from heights, such as from a ladder or from level surfaces; from [electrocution](/wiki/Electrocution "Electrocution") in flooded areas, including from [backfeed](/wiki/Backfeed "Backfeed") from [portable generators](/wiki/Portable_generator "Portable generator"); or from [motor vehicle accidents](/wiki/Motor_vehicle_accidents "Motor vehicle accidents").[[285]](#cite_note-:0-285)[[288]](#cite_note-:1-288)[[289]](#cite_note-289) [Long and irregular shifts](/wiki/Shift_work "Shift work") may lead to [sleep deprivation](/wiki/Sleep_deprivation "Sleep deprivation") and [fatigue](/wiki/Fatigue "Fatigue"), increasing the risk of injuries, and workers may experience [mental stress associated with a traumatic incident](/wiki/Posttraumatic_stress_disorder "Posttraumatic stress disorder"). [Heat stress](/wiki/Heat_stress "Heat stress") is a concern as workers are often exposed to hot and humid temperatures, wear protective clothing and equipment, and have physically difficult tasks.[[285]](#cite_note-:0-285)[[288]](#cite_note-:1-288)

## Extraterrestrial tropical cyclones

See also: [Extraterrestrial vortex](/wiki/Extraterrestrial_vortex "Extraterrestrial vortex")

![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Garcia_et_al._2024_figure_1.webm/250px--Garcia_et_al._2024_figure_1.webm.jpg)

Animated tracks of simulated tropical cyclones on [tidally locked](/wiki/Tidally_locked "Tidally locked") red dwarf exoplanets.

Limited research has been conducted on the possibility of tropical cyclogenesis on other worlds. Polar vortices with structures similar to tropical cyclones have been found on other planets in the [Solar System](/wiki/Solar_System "Solar System"), such as [Venus](/wiki/Venus "Venus")'s north polar vortex and [Saturn's Hexagon](/wiki/Saturn%27s_Hexagon "Saturn's Hexagon").[[290]](#cite_note-290)[[291]](#cite_note-291) The four [giant planets](/wiki/Giant_planet "Giant planet") frequently generate large and extremely powerful storm systems, such as the [Great Red Spot](/wiki/Great_Red_Spot "Great Red Spot") on [Jupiter](/wiki/Jupiter "Jupiter") and the [Great Dark Spots](/wiki/Great_Dark_Spot "Great Dark Spot") on [Neptune](/wiki/Neptune "Neptune"), but these storms are anticyclones.[[292]](#cite_note-Parisi2021-292)[[293]](#cite_note-Polvani1990-293) Tropical cyclones are regarded as a feature unique to Earth.[[294]](#cite_note-Tokano2013-294)

[Titan](/wiki/Titan_(moon) "Titan (moon)"), the largest moon of [Saturn](/wiki/Saturn "Saturn"), is the only other world in the Solar System known to host large bodies of liquid on its surface. It also hosts a "methane cycle," where liquid [hydrocarbons](/wiki/Hydrocarbon "Hydrocarbon") power weather systems much like water does on Earth.[[295]](#cite_note-Battalio2021-295) Despite the appearance of many cloud features, no low-level cyclones have been observed. A 2013 study determined that Titan's tropics are not favorable for tropical cyclogenesis, as its equatorial regions only host isolated lakes and is subject to high wind shear. The Coriolis force is also much weaker on Titan due to its long rotation period (nearly 16 days). However, the study found that hydrocarbon [polar seas](/wiki/Lakes_of_Titan "Lakes of Titan") may contain enough thermal energy to power tropical cyclones. Genesis could be aided by waves generated in the [Seasonal Convergence Zone](/wiki/Climate_of_Titan#Circulation "Climate of Titan") (SCZ), Titan's counterpart to the ITCZ; unlike the ITCZ, the SCZ swings from polar region to polar region as seasons progress. Wind shear in the polar regions is more favorable for tropical cyclogenesis, and the high latitudes in which the polar seas are located could help counter the low Coriolis force to an extent.[[294]](#cite_note-Tokano2013-294)

[Exoplanetary](/wiki/Exoplanet "Exoplanet") climates may be influenced by tropical cyclones, encouraging theoretical analyses of tropical cyclone frequency and distribution on [Earth-like exoplanets](/wiki/Earth_analog "Earth analog").[[296]](#cite_note-Bin2018-296) Earth-sized exoplanets are expected to be common around dim [red dwarf](/wiki/Red_dwarf "Red dwarf") stars;[[297]](#cite_note-297) for these planets to sustain liquid water oceans, they must orbit very close to their parent star.[[298]](#cite_note-298) As a result, many such planets are likely to be [tidally locked](/wiki/Tidal_locking "Tidal locking"), with slower rotation periods and one hemisphere permanently facing the star.[[299]](#cite_note-Yan2020-299) Early planetary climate models with Earth-like atmospheres suggested that tidally locked exoplanets are capable of hosting environments conducive for tropical cyclogenesis, albeit favorability is limited by their slow rotation rates.[[296]](#cite_note-Bin2018-296) Further simulations supported the viability of tropical cyclones on tidally locked planets. A 2020 study found that tropical cyclones are more common for planets near the inner edge of the [habitable zone](/wiki/Habitable_zone "Habitable zone"), with cyclones forming in both the day and night hemispheres on such planets.[[299]](#cite_note-Yan2020-299) A 2024 study further explored the influence of rotation periods on tropical cyclogenesis, concluding that planets with intermediate (8 day) rotation periods are most favorable for tropical cyclogenesis, though weak tropical cyclone-like systems spawned on planets with long (16 day) rotation periods as well.[[300]](#cite_note-Garcia2024-300)

"Data from three center fixes by the Hurricane Hunters indicate that the intensity, based on a blend of 700 mb-flight level and SFMR-observed surface winds, is near 175 kt. This makes Patricia the strongest hurricane on record in the National Hurricane Center's area of responsibility (AOR) which includes the Atlantic and the eastern North Pacific basins."

**[The Hurricane](https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/en:The_Hurricane "s:en:The Hurricane")**

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Wikivoyage-Logo-v3-icon.svg/40px-Wikivoyage-Logo-v3-icon.svg.png)](/wiki/File:Wikivoyage-Logo-v3-icon.svg)

Wikivoyage has a travel guide for ***[Cyclones](https://en.wikivoyage.org/wiki/Cyclones#Q8092 "wikivoyage:Cyclones")***.

* [United States National Hurricane Center](http://www.nhc.noaa.gov/) – North Atlantic, Eastern Pacific
* [United States Central Pacific Hurricane Center](https://web.archive.org/web/20110923144245/http://www.prh.noaa.gov/hnl/cphc/) – Central Pacific
* [Japan Meteorological Agency](http://www.jma.go.jp/en/typh/) – Western Pacific
* [India Meteorological Department](http://www.rsmcnewdelhi.imd.gov.in/index.php?lang=en) – Indian Ocean
* [Météo-France – La Reunion](https://meteofrance.re/fr/cyclone) – South Indian Ocean from 30°E to 90°E
* [Indonesian Meteorological Department](http://meteo.bmkg.go.id/siklon) [Archived](https://web.archive.org/web/20181201104250/http://meteo.bmkg.go.id/siklon/) December 1, 2018, at the [Wayback Machine](/wiki/Wayback_Machine "Wayback Machine") – South Indian Ocean from 90°E to 125°E, north of 10°S
* [Australian Bureau of Meteorology](http://www.bom.gov.au/cyclone/) – South Indian Ocean and South Pacific Ocean from 90°E to 160°E
* [Papua New Guinea National Weather Service](http://www.pngmet.gov.pg/) – South Pacific east of 160°E, north of 10°S
* [Fiji Meteorological Service](http://www.met.gov.fj/) – South Pacific west of 160°E, north of 25° S
* [MetService New Zealand](http://metservice.com/) – South Pacific west of 160°E, south of 25°S
